Refuting the Khawarij on Naming the Sinner
Maturidi refutes the Khawarij by analyzing three possibilities for naming the major sinner.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi systematically refutes the Khawarij position that a major sinner is a disbeliever, by exposing logical contradictions in their reasoning.

Core Idea
Three possibilities, all contradictory: the Khawarij trap themselves.

Imam Maturidi engages with the Khawarij position regarding the status of a Muslim who commits a major sin. Rather than simply rejecting their claim, he systematically examines the possible answers to the question of what such a person should be called. This passage demonstrates the logical force of his refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three possibilities
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice that Maturidi presents three possible answers to the question of how to name the major sinner: (1) he has all faith, (2) he has some faith and some disbelief, (3) he has none.
The Khawarij claim the sinner is a disbeliever; Maturidi tests this by exploring these options.
2
Follow the refutation of each possibility
For each possibility, Maturidi shows how the Khawarij's own logic leads to a contradiction. Pay attention to the arguments he makes, especially the Quranic citation.
The Quranic verses (4:150-151) are used against the second possibility.
3
Understand the overall conclusion
Maturidi concludes that the Khawarij position is self-defeating and rooted in ignorance of God. Reflect on why this refutation is effective.
The Khawarij cannot consistently name the sinner a disbeliever without contradicting themselves.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هل لا تسمونه بما لا يستحق واحدا من الاسمين أو له أحدهما ولا تعلمونه أنتم؟ فإن قالوا بالأول فيقال لهم: أو قد أتى هو بكل الإيمان أو ببعضه، أو لم يأت بشيء، لذلك بطل اسمه؟ فإن قال بالأول أعظم القول ومنع عنه اسم فعله وقد أتى به وجهل بربه حيث لم يحقق لمن تحقق ما له اسمه. ولو جاز ذا لجاز أن لا يكون أحد جاء بالصدق صادقا عند الله في الحقيقة، وكذا كل قائم وقاعد وذو حال لا يجوز عند الله كذلك، أو الله يعلمه كذلك، وعلى ذلك مضادات التي ذكرنا. وهذا آية جهلهم بالله. وإن قال بالثاني فقد شهد الله للذين آمنوا ببعض وكفروا ببعض بأن قالوا: نؤمن ببعض ونكفر ببعض كفارًا حقا، لزمهم التسمية بذلك، وهو رأي الخوارج.
Do you not name him? Is he deserving of one of the two names (believer or disbeliever) or does he have one of them, and you do not know? If they say the first, it is said to them: Did he bring all of faith or some of it, or did he not bring anything, as a result of which his name (of believer) becomes invalid? If he said the first (he brought all), then he has uttered the greatest statement and is prevented from the name of his action, having brought it, and he is ignorant of his Lord because he did not verify for one who verified what his name is. And if this were allowed, it would allow that no one who brings truth is truthful before God in reality, and likewise every standing one, sitting one, and every state would not be permitted before God, or God knows him thus. And based on that are the opposites we mentioned. And this is a sign of their ignorance of God. And if they say the second (some faith), then God has testified for those who believed in some and disbelieved in some when they said: We believe in some and disbelieve in some, they are disbelievers truly. So they must name them thus, and that is the opinion of the Khawarij.
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Khawarij position on naming the perpetrator of a major sin. Maturidi begins by posing a question: when a person commits a major sin but does not commit disbelief (kufr), how should he be named? The Khawarij argue that he is a disbeliever. Maturidi analyzes three possible answers to the question: (1) the person has all of faith and thus should be called a believer, (2) he has some faith and some disbelief, (3) he has none. If the Khawarij say the first, they contradict themselves by denying him the name of believer despite his actions. If they say the second, they are forced to call him a disbeliever, as the Quran does for those who believe in part and disbelieve in part (Q 4:150-151), which is exactly the Khawarij's own view—thus they maintain a contradiction. If they say the third, it is even more absurd, as the one with no faith is even more deserving of disbelief. Maturidi concludes that the Khawarij position is self-defeating and based on ignorance of God.

Key Terms
اسم
مؤمن
كافر
خوارج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Khawarij think their position is consistent: a major sinner is a disbeliever.
Correction: Maturidi shows that their position leads to a contradiction because the only consistent classification from their reasoning is that the person has some faith and some disbelief, which the Quran calls disbelief, so they are essentially saying the sinner is a disbeliever—but that itself is inconsistent with the Muslim consensus.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, which of the three possible responses leads to the Khawarij position?
A) The person has all of faith
B) The person has some faith and some disbelief
C) The person has none of faith
D) The person's state is unknown

Key Takeaway
The Khawarij view leads to self-contradiction because the only consistent position based on their logic is the one that equates partial belief with disbelief, which the Quran itself condemns.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
This passage shows that even partial faith prevents branding someone a disbeliever. Is there a person you have already written off as 'lost' or 'not a real Muslim'? What would it take for you to see them as having some faith?
?
The Khawarij were certain they were right, yet Maturidi shows their position collapses. In what area of your own understanding of Islam are you dangerously certain? What would happen if you examined it as rigorously?

The First Proof: God's Binary Division of Humankind
Maturidi presents the first proof from God's division of humanity and rebukes the Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's first proof that God divides humanity into only two categories—believers and disbelievers—and why the Mu'tazila's three-fold division is a transgression.

Core Idea
God's own classification of humanity into two is the standard; adding a third is transgression.

Imam Maturidi begins by recalling a fundamental principle: God has divided humankind into two categories for both this world and the Hereafter. The Mu'tazila, however, introduced a third category for the major sinner who is not a believer but not a disbeliever. In this passage, Maturidi argues that this innovation violates God's established limit, and he challenges them with Quranic rhetorical questions.

Guided Path
1
Recall the Context
Remember that the issue is about naming the major sinner. Maturidi is supporting the correct principle.
The principle likely states that the major sinner is either a believer or a disbeliever with no middle state.
2
Examine the First Proof
Read the passage where Maturidi invokes God's division of humans into two categories.
3
Understand the Quranic Challenge
Maturidi quotes Quranic rhetorical questions to rebuke the Mu'tazila. Compare their division to transgression.
The verses are similar to those addressed to the Jews.
4
Reflect on the Implication
Consider why this proof is strong: God's own classification leaves no room for a third category.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيد هذا الأصل وجهان. أحدهما ما ذكرت من قسمة الله البشر قسمين في أمر الدنيا والآخرة؛ فقسمة المعتزلة على ثلاثة أقسام تعد لحد الله، وحق مثله أن يقال له: «الله أذن لكم أم على الله تفترون»، أو يقال: «أأنتم أعلم أم الله» كما قيل لليهود. والثاني أن الله تعالى نفي الإيمان في محكم تنزيله عن قوم على تحقيق الكفر، إذ قال: وما أولئك بالمؤمنين، ولم يخطر ببال عاقل أنهم لعلهم ليسوا بكفار؛ بل إذا أزيل الإيمان عمن يكون له فعل الإيمان: فإنما يزال بالكفر. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وإن قالوا: لا نعلم له أحد الاسمين وله ذلك عند الله كفوا موؤنة الجدل، لأن ما لا يعلمونه أكثر مما يحصى، لو لزم محاجتهم فيها ليذهب العمر باطلاً. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Two aspects support this principle. One of them is what I mentioned: God's division of humankind into two categories in matters of this world and the Hereafter. So the Mu'tazila's division into three categories is a transgression against God's limit. It is right to say to them: 'Did God permit you or do you invent lies against God?' or 'Are you more knowing or is God?' as was said to the Jews. The second is that God Almighty negated faith in His decisive revelation from a people while affirming disbelief, when He said: 'And those are not the believers,' and no rational person would think that perhaps they are not disbelievers. Rather, when faith is removed from one who has the action of faith, it is only removed by disbelief. There is no power except through God. And if they say: We do not know that he has one of the two names, and that belongs to God alone, they would spare themselves the burden of argument, because what they do not know is more than what can be counted; if they were compelled to argue about it, life would be wasted in vain. There is no power except through God.
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage provides two proofs to support the correct principle regarding naming the major sinner. The first proof is based on God's division of humankind into two categories—believers and disbelievers—in both this world and the Hereafter. The Mu'tazila introduce a third category (the fasiq in an intermediate position), which Maturidi argues is a violation of God's classification. He cites Quranic verses challenging those who invent divisions without divine permission. The second proof is from the Quran itself, where God negates faith from a people while at the same time affirming their disbelief. This indicates that the removal of faith is equivalent to disbelief; there is no intermediate state. Maturidi then addresses a potential objection: if someone says they do not know the person's status and leave it to God, this is a cop-out, as it would make all debate impossible. The principle is thus established on scripture and reason.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila's threefold division is a reasonable middle ground.
Correction: Maturidi shows it is a transgression of God's established division.

A) A logical innovation
B) A transgression of God's limit
C) An unknown matter
D) A valid interpretation

Key Takeaway
God's division of humanity into believers and disbelievers is definitive; any third category is a transgression.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The Mu'tazila thought reason allowed an intermediate position. What in your life have you rationalized as 'not that bad' even though God has clearly commanded or prohibited it? Are you willing to stop justifying and submit to His binary judgment?
?
God's division is final. Are you living as if you can decide for yourself where you stand? Will you today accept His classification and take concrete action to align your deeds with being a true believer?

The Second Proof: Negation of Faith Implies Disbelief
Maturidi gives the second proof from Quranic negation of faith and refutes agnosticism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's second proof that when God negates faith from a people, it simultaneously affirms their disbelief, and you will see how he refutes the claim that we cannot judge.

Core Idea
Removing faith from someone is equivalent to affirming disbelief; there is no middle state.

Imam Maturidi presents his second proof, which is directly from the Quran. God says about certain people: 'And those are not the believers.' No rational person would doubt that they are disbelievers. Maturidi then addresses an objection: if someone says we don't know the person's status and leave it to God, this would destroy all theological discourse.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage Carefully
Read the Arabic and translation of the second proof. Note the Quranic quotation and the logical statement that follows.
2
Understand the Logical Implication
Maturidi argues that removing faith from a person who has faith actions can only be done by disbelief. Why is that necessary?
This is a key principle: faith and disbelief are opposites with no middle.
3
Consider the Objection and Response
The objection claims we don't know the person's name (believer/disbeliever) and it's for God alone. Maturidi replies that this attitude would make all debate impossible.
Notice the rhetorical weight of 'life would be wasted in vain.'
4
Reflect on the Overall Argument
See how the second proof, combined with the first, firmly establishes that there is no third status for the major sinner.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Two aspects support this principle. One of them is what I mentioned: God's division of humankind into two categories in matters of this world and the Hereafter. So the Mu'tazila's division into three categories is a transgression against God's limit. It is right to say to them: 'Did God permit you or do you invent lies against God?' or 'Are you more knowing or is God?' as was said to the Jews. The second is that God Almighty negated faith in His decisive revelation from a people while affirming disbelief, when He said: 'And those are not the believers,' and no rational person would think that perhaps they are not disbelievers. Rather, when faith is removed from one who has the action of faith, it is only removed by disbelief. There is no power except through God. And if they say: We do not know that he has one of the two names, and that belongs to God alone, they would spare themselves the burden of argument, because what they do not know is more than what can be counted; if they were compelled to argue about it, life would be wasted in vain. There is no power except through God.
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage provides two proofs to support the correct principle regarding naming the major sinner. The first proof is based on God's division of humankind into two categories—believers and disbelievers—in both this world and the Hereafter. The Mu'tazila introduce a third category (the fasiq in an intermediate position), which Maturidi argues is a violation of God's classification. He cites Quranic verses challenging those who invent divisions without divine permission. The second proof is from the Quran itself, where God negates faith from a people while at the same time affirming their disbelief. This indicates that the removal of faith is equivalent to disbelief; there is no intermediate state. Maturidi then addresses a potential objection: if someone says they do not know the person's status and leave it to God, this is a cop-out, as it would make all debate impossible. The principle is thus established on scripture and reason.

Key Terms
مؤمن
كافر
إيمان
كفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: We cannot know whether a major sinner is a believer or disbeliever, so we should remain agnostic.
Correction: Maturidi shows that Quranic evidence determines it, and agnosticism is impractical.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does the removal of faith from a person entail?
A) They become a fasiq
B) They become a disbeliever
C) They remain in an intermediate state
D) Their status is unknown

Key Takeaway
Negation of faith from a person is equivalent to affirming their disbelief; there is no third option.

Consensus on Naming the Major Sinner
Maturidi cites the consensus of the Ummah that the major sinner has a religious name.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the consensus of the Ummah that a major sinner must be given a religious name and why nullifying this consensus is invalid.

Core Idea
The Ummah's consensus on naming the sinner is a binding foundation.

Maturidi now turns to the consensus of the Muslim community—despite their internal disagreements—on a key point: every major sinner has a name that belongs to one of the religious categories. This passage explains why avoiding such naming out of caution actually contradicts what the Quran and Sunnah affirm. Let's see how Maturidi argues from consensus and revelation.

The reference to 'giving ear and being a witness' or 'having a heart' (Q 50:37) indicates that the truth of this matter is clear to those who are attentive and have understanding.

Maturidi's Refutation of Mu'tazila on Fasiq and Fajir
Maturidi criticizes the Mu'tazila for inventing the names fasiq and fajir for the major sinner.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's criticism of the Mu'tazila for introducing the terms 'fasiq' and 'fajir' as a third category for the major sinner, and why he considers this an innovation.

Core Idea
The names for the major sinner come from revelation; inventing new names is bid'a.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi takes issue with the Mu'tazila's classification of the major sinner as a 'fasiq' or 'fajir'. While other groups either affirm his faith or declare him a disbeliever, the Mu'tazila devised a middle ground. Maturidi argues that this is an innovation, as the scriptural names are clear. Let's examine his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the meaning.
Notice the contrast between the positions mentioned.
2
Identify the three positions
The passage mentions those who call the major sinner a disbeliever, those who call him a believer, and the Mu'tazila who invented new names. Find each.
3
Reflect on the innovation accusation
Maturidi says the Mu'tazila innovated (أبدعت) these names. Consider why he views this as a problem: the names should come from revelation, not human invention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم القول بالفاسق والفاجر مطلقا مما يُتوزّع فيه؛ ومن سماه كافرا أو مشركا أطلقه، ومن سماه مؤمنًا أبي ذلك. وكذلك جحدوا اسم أعداء الله؛ وأبدعت المعتزلة هذين الاسمين على ذينك الاسمين خلافا لما عليه الأمر. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then the statement about 'fasiq' and 'fajir' in an absolute sense is a matter of dispute. Whoever names him a disbeliever or a polytheist has made an absolute statement, and whoever names him a believer has refused that. Likewise, they denied the name of God's enemies (disbelievers). And the Mu'tazila innovated these two names (fasiq and fajir) in place of those two names (kufr and shirk), contrary to what the matter is. There is no power except through God.
Source pages p. 429

Commentary
This passage addresses the Mu'tazila's position on naming the major sinner. While others either call him a believer (Murji'a) or a disbeliever (Khawarij), the Mu'tazila introduced the terms 'fasiq' (immoral) and 'fajir' (sinner) as a middle ground. Maturidi criticizes this as an innovation (bid'a) because it replaces the established names of kufr and shirk that are derived from revelation. He points out that there is no consensus on using these terms absolutely for the major sinner; they are disputed. By denying the names of God's enemies (the disbelievers) and inventing new names, the Mu'tazila have deviated from the clear scriptural classifications.

Correction: Maturidi argues that 'fasiq' and 'fajir' are not from revelation but were innovated by the Mu'tazila, thus they are not valid scriptural categories for the major sinner.

Refuting Khawarij and Mu'tazila on Forgiveness
Maturidi refutes Khawarij and Mu'tazila interpretations of key verses on forgiveness and expiation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi refutes the Khawarij and Mu'tazila misinterpretations of Quranic verses on forgiveness and expiation.

Core Idea
Misinterpreting forgiveness verses leads to flawed theology; context and sin categories matter.

Imam Maturidi engages with two controversial sectarian interpretations of key Quranic verses. The Khawarij and Mu'tazila had misread verses on shirk and major sins to support their doctrines. In this passage, Maturidi systematically shows the logical and textual flaws in their readings. You will see how careful attention to Arabic phrasing and theological consistency exposes these errors.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice how Maturidi addresses two verses and two groups.
The passage is dense; reading slowly helps.
2
Identify the two verses and positions
The Khawarij interpret Q 4:48 and Q 4:31 in a way that conflates sins. Identify their claim and Maturidi's critique.
Q 4:48 is about shirk; Q 4:31 is about avoiding major sins.
3
Understand the Mu'tazila refutation
Maturidi briefly refutes the Mu'tazila, saying their position prevents proper understanding of similarity between sins.
What does 'similarity' (al-shabah) refer to? It likely means the Mu'tazila fail to see the distinction between major and minor sins.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قوله: إن الله لا يغفر أن يشرك به الآية، تأويل الخوارج فيه «من الخطأ» فاسد، لأنه ليس بذنب فيغفر، وفي هذا ذكر المغفرة؛ ولا يُحتمل إضمار التوبة لما يغفر بمثله الشرك، والآية في التمييز بين الذنبين. وكذلك لا يحتمل قوله: إن تجتنبوا كبائر ما تنهون عنه الآية، لما فيه التكفير، وما لا ذنب لا يكفّر، والخطأ لا يحقق الذنب، والتكفير يكون لشيء يُجزى به. ولا يحتمل ما قالت المعتزلة لأن قولهم يمنع تحقيق الشبه.
Then His saying: 'Indeed, God does not forgive that partners be ascribed to Him' [the verse], the interpretation of the Khawarij therein regarding 'from error' is corrupt, because it is not a sin that can be forgiven—and in this is the mention of forgiveness—and it is not possible to imply repentance for something like which shirk is forgiven. And the verse is about distinguishing between the two types of sin. Likewise, His saying: 'If you avoid the major sins that you are forbidden from' [the verse] is not possible, because in it there is expiation, and there is no sin to be expiated, and error does not constitute a sin, and expiation applies to something deserving recompense. And what the Mu'tazila said is not possible, because their statement prevents the verification of similarity.
Source pages p. 429

Commentary
This passage critiques the interpretations of two Quranic verses by the Khawarij and Mu'tazila. The Khawarij take the verse 'God does not forgive shirk' (Q 4:48) to imply that all major sins are shirk and thus unforgivable unless repented. Maturidi argues that this interpretation is corrupt: the verse distinguishes shirk from other sins, and it explicitly mentions forgiveness being withheld for shirk, implying that other sins can be forgiven. The idea that the verse implies repentance for shirk-like sins is not supported. The second verse (Q 4:31) states that avoiding major sins leads to expiation of smaller sins. The Khawarij here again err by claiming that major sins committed in error are not sins. Maturidi points out that expiation applies only to actual sins, not errors. The Mu'tazila are also refuted because their position prevents the correct assessment of similarity between sins. The passage defends the distinction between major sins and shirk, and between sins and errors.

خوارج
معتزلة
مغفرة
تكفير
خطأ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Khawarij interpretation of Q 4:48 is correct: all major sins are shirk and unforgivable.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the verse distinguishes shirk from other sins, so not all major sins are unforgivable.

Two Types of Major Sins and Avoidance by Belief
Maturidi classifies major sins into two types and explains avoidance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's classification of major sins into belief-based and action-based, and how avoidance is defined primarily by one's belief.

Core Idea
Major sins are of two types: those in belief and those in actions; avoidance is a matter of conviction.

Maturidi begins by clarifying the nature of major sins (kaba'ir). He divides them into two categories: sins related to belief (i'tiqad) and sins related to actions (af'al). This distinction allows him to explain how one can be considered 'avoiding' major sins even if one commits the act, as long as one's belief recognizes the sin as grave. He then applies this interpretation to the Qur'anic verse on avoiding major sins.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let Maturidi's own classification come through before you interpret.
Notice that Maturidi uses a very concise style.
2
Identify the two types of major sins
Find the two categories Maturidi mentions: 'كبائر في الاعتقاد' and 'كبائر الأفعال'. Note what each includes.
The first type is disbelief and denial, the second type is sinful actions.
3
Understand avoidance by belief
Maturidi says that a person who commits action-based major sins can still be 'avoiding' them if his belief recognizes them as great sins. Ask yourself: How does this differ from simply refraining from the act?
This is the key insight: avoidance is primarily a matter of conviction, not just action.
4
Apply to the verse
Maturidi then interprets the Qur'anic verse about avoiding major sins as referring primarily to avoiding belief-based major sins (polytheism). Reflect on how this interpretation resolves potential contradictions.
He connects this to verses about expiation and forgiveness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني أن الكبائر نوعان أحدهما كبائر في الاعتقاد من أنواع الكفر والتكذيب التي بها اختلفت الكفرة، وأخرى كبائر الأفعال التي صاحبها مجتنب عنها بالاعتقاد في أن يراها على ما جعلها الله عليه من عظم الفعل والذنب ؛ وهذا اجتناب وقد يواقعها بالفعل، وهو الارتكاب، وليس في الآية وجها الاجتناب. فجائز أن يكون: إن يجتنب كبائر الاعتقاد، وهي أنواع الشرك، يُكفّر عنه دونها لمن يشاء بما شاء من غير ذلك بالحسنات أو بالتفضل، كما بينا في إحدى الآيتين بالتكفير وفي الأخرى بالمغفرة. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And the second [point] is that major sins are of two types: one type are major sins in belief, from the types of disbelief and denial by which the disbelievers differ; and another type are major sins of actions, whose doer is avoiding them by belief in that he sees them as God has made them for him of the greatness of the act and the sin. This is avoidance, and he may commit them in action, and that is commission. And there is not in the verse a face of avoidance. So it is possible that it means: if they avoid the major sins of belief, which are the types of polytheism, He will expiate for them [their minor sins] besides them for whomever He wills, as He wills, by good deeds or by grace, as we explained in one of the two verses regarding expiation and in the other regarding forgiveness. And there is no power except through God.
Source pages p. 431

Commentary
This passage is a classification of major sins. Maturidi states that major sins (kaba'ir) are of two types: those in belief (i'tiqad), which are the various kinds of disbelief and denial that differentiate disbelievers, and those in actions (af'al). For the latter, a person can be considered 'avoiding' them by his belief – that is, he recognizes them as great sins as God has determined, and thus he avoids them in his conviction, even if he sometimes commits them in action. This distinction allows Maturidi to interpret the verse about avoiding major sins (Qur'an 4:31) as referring primarily to avoiding belief-based major sins (i.e., polytheism). If one avoids those, God may forgive lesser sins (duna-ha) by good deeds or grace, as indicated in other verses. He then invokes 'La hawla wa la quwwata illa billah' to conclude.

Key Terms
الكبائر
الاعتقاد
الاجتناب
الارتكاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that avoiding major sins requires never committing them; any sin means one is not avoiding.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that avoidance can be by belief even if one commits the sinful act, as long as one's belief recognizes the sin as grave.

Checkpoint

A) Sins of ignorance and sins of heedlessness
B) Sins in belief and sins of actions
C) Sins of the heart and sins of the tongue
D) Sins before prophethood and sins after

Key Takeaway
Major sins are of two kinds, and avoidance is primarily a matter of belief and recognition, not just action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Scale of Punishments for Major Sins
Maturidi argues that punishment for shirk is eternal, not for lesser sins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that the punishment for shirk is eternal, while major sins of believers are not.

Core Idea
Punishment fits the crime: shirk deserves eternity; belief erases sins.

In this passage, Maturidi addresses a crucial question: are all major sins punished equally? He argues that the Qur'anic principle of requiting evil with its like means that polytheism and obstinacy—which are directed against God Himself—deserve eternal punishment. However, for a believer who lacks these traits, the very soundness of their faith can expiate lesser sins. This distinction is central to Maturidi's theology of divine justice and mercy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the key terms: التخليد, المعاندة, الشرك, صحة الاعتقاد.
2
Identify the principle of reciprocity
Maturidi begins by stating that God has not specified the exact punishment for each major sin. But he then invokes a known principle: evil deeds are recompensed only with their like. What is the 'like' of polytheism and obstinacy?
The answer is eternal punishment (التخليد).
3
Understand the role of sound belief
Maturidi contrasts the obstinate polytheist with the person who has sound belief. This belief leads to fear and hope, and it can erase sins if it is dominant. Why does this prevent equal punishment?
Because the sin of the believer is not of the same magnitude as that of the disbeliever.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالث أنه لم يبيَّن في الكبائر قدر العقوبات. ومعلوم أن الله نفي أن يجزى في السيئات إلا مثلها. ومثل الشرك والمعاندة إنما هو التخليد ولا ريب أن من ليس بمعاند ولا مشرك له في العبادة ذنبه دون من يفعل ذلك ؛ بل صحة الاعتقاد في ذلك حَمَلَه على الخوف مما حذره ورجاء ما أطمعه في الاعتقاد، وهو الذي لو سبق كل شيء لكفّره هو ومحاه عنه. لم يجز أن يكون قدر ذنبه قدر الأول، فلم يجز أن يخلّد في ذلك.
And the third [point] is that the measure of punishments for major sins was not explained. And it is known that God denied that anyone be recompensed in evil deeds except with its like. And the like of polytheism and obstinacy is only eternal punishment; and there is no doubt that he who is not obstinate nor polytheist has in worship a sin less than the one who does that. Rather, the soundness of belief in that caused him to fear what He warned against and to hope for what He made him desire in belief, and it is that which if it preceded everything, would expiate it and erase it from him. It is not permissible that the measure of his sin be the measure of the first, so it is not permissible that he be eternally punished in that.
Source pages p. 431

Commentary
This passage is the third argument in a series. Maturidi states that God has not specified the exact punishments for each major sin. However, it is a known principle (ma'lum) that God only recompenses evil with its like (Qur'an 6:160). Therefore, the 'like' of polytheism (shirk) and obstinacy ('inad) must be eternal punishment (takhled). Consequently, the person who is neither obstinate nor polytheist has a lesser sin than the obstinate polytheist. Moreover, the soundness of belief (sihhat al-i'tiqad) in that person leads him to fear God's warnings and hope for His promises – and this very belief, if it had preceded everything, would have expiated and erased the sin. Thus, his sin cannot be equal to that of the first (the obstinate disbeliever), and he should not be eternally punished.

Key Terms
التخليد
المعاندة
الشرك
صحة الاعتقاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All major sins lead to eternal punishment regardless of faith.
Correction: Maturidi argues that only polytheism and obstinacy merit eternal punishment; for believers, soundness of belief can expiate sins.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi argue that the sin of a polytheist and a non-polytheist cannot be equal?
A) Because God's mercy is greater for believers
B) Because the soundness of belief in the non-polytheist would expiate lesser sins
C) Because the polytheist's sin is only in action, not belief
D) Because God did not specify punishments for major sins

Key Takeaway
The measure of punishment is based on the presence or absence of obstinate disbelief, and sound belief can expiate lesser sins.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly fear God's warning and hope for His promise, or do you rely on a vague belief that automatically erases your sins without personal accountability? When was the last time you felt that your sin was expiated because of your belief, and what change did that bring?
?
If your belief is sound enough to erase sins, why do you still commit the same minor sins repeatedly? Is it because you underestimate God's justice or overestimate your own faith?
?
The passage says sound belief causes fear and hope. Which one dominates your heart right now: fear of God's punishment or hope in His mercy? And how does that affect your actions today?

Second Matter: Greater Good of Iman Outweighs Sin
Maturidi argues that eternal punishment of believers would be unjust because it nullifies the greater good of iman.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that eternal punishment of believers would be injustice because it annuls the immense good of iman.

Core Idea
Eternal punishment of believers would contradict God's own justice which multiplies good deeds but punishes evil only equally.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi presents a compelling argument about divine justice. He compares the immense good of iman with the lesser evil of sin, arguing that if God were to eternally punish a believer for their sins, He would effectively cancel out the reward of the greatest good — faith itself. This, he says, would be a description of injustice, not justice. Let's examine his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Pay attention to the comparison Maturidi makes between the good of iman and the evil of sin.
2
Identify the key comparison
Notice how Maturidi says the good that confronts denial is greater than the good from obedience. He is establishing a hierarchy of good.
This sets up his argument.
3
Understand the conclusion
Trace how Maturidi concludes that eternal punishment would be injustice. He points to God's own principle of multiplying good deeds and punishing evil only like-for-like.
This is the core of his reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني أنه معلوم أن الذي يقابل الجحود والمعاندة من الخير أعظم وأجل من الذي يقابل ما كان في قبوله أن يفعل من الترك، على نحو ما كان الجحود والمعاندة من الآخر. فجاء بالذي هو في الخير أعظم الخير، وفي الشر لم يبلغ نهايته. فإذا خلّده في النار أبطل ثواب أفضل الخيرات بارتكاب ما دونه من الشر. ومَرَدّ ذلك وصف الجور لا العدل. والعدل أن يزيد في ثواب ما جاء به على عقاب ما أتى به والله جل ثناؤه قد أخبر أنه يجزى الحسنة بعشرة أمثالها، والسيئة بمثلها، وفي هذا لم يبلغ المثل في الحسنة ولا قصر على المثل في السيئة، جل الله عن ذلك وتعالى.
And the second [matter] is that it is known that what confronts denial and obstinacy from good is greater and more majestic than what confronts what was in its acceptance of doing from omission, in the manner that denial and obstinacy were from the other. So he brought what is in good the greatest good, and in evil he did not reach its extreme. So if He eternally punishes him in the Fire, He nullifies the reward of the best good deeds by committing what is lesser than it from evil. And the return of that is the description of injustice, not justice. And justice is that He should increase the reward of what he brought over the punishment of what he did. And God, exalted be His mention, has informed that He rewards the good deed with ten times its like, and the evil deed with its like, and in this He did not reach the like in the good deed nor restrict to the like in the evil deed. Exalted is God above that and highly exalted.
Source pages p. 432

Commentary
This is the second of the two matters. Maturidi argues that the good (khayr) that comes from denial (juhud) and obstinacy (mu'anada) is actually greater and more majestic than the good that comes from acceptance of obedience that is sometimes omitted. The good of iman is the greatest good, while the evil of sin does not reach that extreme. So if God were to eternally punish a believer who commits sins, He would be annulling the reward of the best good deeds (i.e., iman) because of lesser evils. That would be describing injustice (jawr), not justice ('adl). Justice requires that the reward for good deeds outweigh the punishment for evil deeds, as God Himself declared: good deeds are multiplied tenfold, while evil deeds are only punished like-for-like. In the case of the believer, God's treatment does not even reach the like in the good deed (i.e., He gives more) and does not restrict to the like in the evil deed (i.e., He may forgive). Thus, eternal punishment for such a person would be contrary to God's own principles.

Key Terms
الخير
الشر
العدل
الجور
ثواب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that justice demands equal punishment for each sin regardless of the person's faith.
Correction: Maturidi explains that justice requires considering the greater good of iman; punishing a believer eternally would nullify that immense good and thus be injustice.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what would it mean if God eternally punishes a believer for his sins?
A) Justice, because sin must be punished
B) Injustice, because it annuls the greater good of iman
C) Mercy, because it purifies the soul
D) Wisdom, because it deters others

Key Takeaway
Eternal punishment of believers would nullify the reward of iman and contradict God's own principle of multiplying good deeds, thus it is impossible for a just God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that your faith in Allah outweighs any sin you commit, why do you still live in fear of your sins more than in hope of Allah’s mercy? What would change in your daily life if you internalized this principle?
?
When you condemn others for their sins, do you consider the weight of their iman as Maturidi insists? How can you practice justice like Allah, giving weight to both the good and the evil of others?
?
The passage says eternal punishment would nullify the reward of iman. Have you ever treated your own iman lightly, as if it can be canceled by a sin? What is one concrete step you can take today to honor the greatness of your faith?

Why Sin Does Not Prove Initial Faith Was False
Maturidi refutes the argument that sin proves initial faith false using two aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of the claim that committing a major sin proves one's initial faith was insincere, including the two aspects of his argument.

Core Idea
A later sin does not reveal a lie in one's initial faith.

Maturidi addresses a common argument that a person's subsequent sin exposes his earlier faith as false. He refutes this with logical reasoning, showing that internal sincerity is known to the individual and Allah alone. This passage presents two key aspects of his refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the full translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the two 'وجهان' (aspects) mentioned towards the end.
2
Identify the two aspects
Locate where Maturidi says 'وجهان' and note what each aspect argues. The first involves logical consequences; the second involves self-knowledge and divine knowledge.
3
Trace the first aspect's logic
Follow the conditional reasoning: if sin proved insincerity, then the necessity of avoiding sin would be removed, and absurd consequences follow (e.g., no difference between apostate and original disbeliever).
This shows the argument leads to contradictions.
4
Examine the second aspect
Understand that every person knows his own sincerity at the time of belief, and Allah knows the reality. Therefore, sin cannot be used to disprove that knowledge.
The claimant who says otherwise is himself lying.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that the one who makes this claim is guilty of lying and deserves judgment of disbelief. Consider how this strengthens the refutation.
This ties back to the core idea: actions do not retroactively invalidate sincere faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واستدلال من استدل بترك الفعل على الكذب في الأول محال فاسد، لأن في عقل كل واحد لزوم اتقاء الكذب كما في القبول إبقاؤه . ثم لم يصر وجوده دليلاً على كذب عقله، لما في عقله منعه . وإن تعدى ذلك فمثله في قبول وقت تعديه . ولو كان في ذلك تبيّن لكان كل شيء إذ حل بالأول، يجب فساده وحرمته . ولو كان كذلك لكان لا يجب على المرتد خلاف على الكافر الأصلي بل بتصريح الكفر لا يظهر كذبه في الأول، فكيف فيما فعل ؟ ولو كان بذا ذا، فيظهر أيضاً بإيمان الكافر من بعد أو بتعاطيه ما لا يقبح في العرف تصديقه على كذبه وأصله وجهان أحدهما لو كان يظهر به الكذب في الأول لأزيل اللزوم، وإذا زال تعدى هذا ليبطل أن يصير وجوده سببًا لإظهار ذلك ، وفيه بطلان ما قالوا. ولا قوة إلا بالله. والثاني أن كلاً يعلم من نفسه في وقت اعتقاده أنه غير كاذب في ذلك، ثم يعلمه أيضا من في دينه. ولو كان به ظهور لكان لا علم يقع في الحقيقة . ولا قوة إلا بالله. بل مدعي يلزمه ذلك ، إذ في اعتقاده أن لا يكذب . وكل مؤمن يعلم أنه بهذا القول كاذب، وكذلك الله سبحانه، إذ هو يعلم حقيقة كل شيء بما هو عليه لا بغيره، يعلم صدقه في الأول، وإن تعدى من بعده ؛ فيصير صاحب هذا القول عند الله وعند من شهد عليه بالكذب كاذبًا، فيستوجب به من كل أحد القضاء عليه بالكفر في قوله الذي يريد به تثبيت كفر غيره. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And the deduction: whoever deduces from the commission of an act [i.e., sin] lying in the first [faith] is invalid and corrupt, because in the intellect of every individual is the necessity of avoiding lying just as in acceptance its preservation. Then its existence did not become evidence for the lying of his intellect, because in his intellect is its prevention. And if he transgresses that, then its like is in acceptance at the time of his transgression. And if in that there were clarification, then every thing, when it occurs in the first, would necessitate its corruption and prohibition. And if it were so, then it would not be incumbent upon the apostate differently from the original disbeliever; rather, by explicit disbelief, his lying in the first does not appear, so how about what he did? And if it were so, then it would also appear, by the faith of the disbeliever later or by his engaging in what is not considered ugly in custom, his confirmation upon his lying. And its origin has two aspects: one of them is that if lying were to appear by it in the first, the necessity would be removed, and when it is removed, this is extended to nullify that its existence becomes a cause for manifesting that, and in it is the invalidity of what they said. And there is no power except with Allah. And the second: that everyone knows from himself at the time of his belief that he is not lying in that, then also knows him who is in his religion. And if there were manifestation by it, then no knowledge would occur in reality. And there is no power except with Allah. Rather, the claimant is obligated by that, since in his belief is that he does not lie. And every believer knows that he is by this statement a liar, and likewise Allah, the Exalted, for He knows the reality of everything as it is, not by other than it. He knows his truthfulness in the first, even if he transgresses after that. So the holder of this statement becomes, in the sight of Allah and in the sight of those who testified against him of lying, a liar, thus deserving by it from everyone the judgment of disbelief upon his statement by which he intends to establish the disbelief of others. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 432

Commentary
This passage is a logical refutation of the argument that committing a major sin proves that one's initial faith was insincere. Maturidi provides two aspects (وجهان). First, if sin were proof of lying in the initial faith, then the obligation to avoid sin would be removed, and also it would lead to absurd consequences like not distinguishing between apostate and original disbeliever. Second, each person knows his own sincerity at the time of belief, and Allah knows the truth, so the claim that sin reveals lying is false. The one who makes such a claim is himself guilty of lying and deserves judgment of disbelief.

Key Terms
استدلال
كذب
لزوم
وجهان
اعتقاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A person's later actions, especially major sins, reveal that his earlier faith was insincere.
Correction: Maturidi argues that internal sincerity is known only to the individual and Allah, and that sin does not retroactively prove insincerity.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is one reason why committing a sin does not prove that one's initial faith was insincere?
A) Because the person knows his own sincerity
B) Because sin is always forgivable
C) Because the intellect prevents lying
D) Both A and C

Key Takeaway
Maturidi establishes that later sin cannot invalidate the sincerity of one's initial faith, as sincerity is known to the individual and Allah alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you have fallen into sin after a moment of sincere faith, have you ever doubted whether your initial faith was genuine? How does Maturidi's argument that self-knowledge at the time of belief is reliable challenge that doubt?
?
Imagine someone dear to you commits a grave sin after years of practicing Islam. Do you find yourself questioning the sincerity of their past faith? This passage says such judgment is akin to calling them a liar in the sight of Allah—how does that weight on your heart?
?
If you were to die tonight in a state of sin, would you still be certain that your faith was true? Maturidi points out that Allah knows your truthfulness in the first, even after transgression. What would it take for you to rest in that knowledge—or does it unsettle you?

Quranic Proof That Sin Does Not Negate Faith
Maturidi cites Quran 4:137 to refute Mu'tazila on sin and faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi uses Quran 4:137 to argue that sin does not negate faith and his refutation of the Mu'tazila position on grave sins.

Core Idea
Even repeated disbelief does not erase faith's establishment; sin cannot negate it.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi turns to the Quran to counter the view that committing sins removes faith. He cites a verse about those who believe and disbelieve repeatedly to show that faith is still affirmed at times, thereby undermining the Mu'tazila claim that grave sins expel one from faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the Quranic citation and the logical moves Maturidi makes.
Pay attention to the structure: citation, conditional argument, application to Mu'tazila, and critique.
2
Identify the Quranic verse
Find the verse (4:137) and understand its context. Maturidi uses it to show that faith is established even after disbelief.
The verse mentions multiple cycles of belief and disbelief.
3
Understand the logical argument
Maturidi argues: if the Mu'tazila were right that sin negates faith, then these people would never have had faith at all. But the verse affirms faith at times, so their position is false.
He then extends the argument: if this holds for disbelief, how much more for lesser sins?
4
See the application to Mu'tazila
Maturidi explicitly applies this reasoning to the Mu'tazila doctrine regarding grave sins (kaba'ir).
He also points out the contradiction in calling grave sinners 'people of prayer and qibla' while denying they have faith.
5
Reflect on the key takeaway
The passage establishes that sin does not negate faith; faith remains even in the presence of major sins.
This is a core Maturidi position against the Mu'tazila.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله تعالى : إن الذين آمنوا ثم كفروا ثم آمنوا ثم كفروا ، ولو كان في ذلك ما ذكر لم يكن ليثبت لهم إيمان أبدا، فثبت به فساد قولهم. فهذا في الكفر ، فكيف فيهما دونه. ولا قوة إلا بالله . / وعلى ذلك اختلاف الأحوال في الخلق لا يوجب فساد مضاداتها في غير تلك الأحوال وبالله التوفيق. وهذا أيضاً يلزم المعتزلة في الكبائر . ثم العجب من هؤلاء يثبتون لأصحاب الكبائر اسم أهل الصلاة و القبلة؛ وسبب إثبات هذا الاسم لهم الإيمان، فمحال زواله على بقاء ذلك، وما به ثبت قد زال. والله أعلم. وقد روى في الآية القراءة على إن تجتنبوا كبير ما تنهون عنه ، وإن كان المعروف ذلك ، فإنه قد يجوز إرادة الآحاد بحرف الجمع، فلا ننكر أن تكون الآية على ذلك
And the saying of the Exalted: 'Indeed, those who have believed then disbelieved, then believed, then disbelieved...' [4:137] And if in that were what they mentioned, then faith would never be established for them. So the corruption of their statement is established. So this is concerning disbelief, so how about what is less than it? And there is no power except with Allah. And upon that, the variation of states in creation does not necessitate the corruption of their opposites in other than those states. And with Allah is success. And this also necessitatedly applies to the Mu'tazila concerning grave sins. Then the wonder from these people: they affirm for the perpetrators of grave sins the name of 'people of prayer and qibla', and the cause of affirming this name for them is faith, so it is impossible for it to disappear while that remains, and that by which it was established has disappeared. And Allah knows best. And it has been narrated concerning the verse the reading: 'If you avoid the great (kabir) of what He forbids you' [4:31], and even if the known is that [plural], then it may be permissible to intend singular by the plural letter, so we do not deny that the verse is upon that.
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage uses Quranic evidence from 4:137 to show that even those who repeatedly believe and disbelieve are described as having faith at some point, so committing sins does not negate faith. Maturidi then applies this to the Mu'tazila who hold that grave sins exclude from faith. He then notes the contradiction of those who still call grave sinners 'people of prayer and qibla' while denying them faith. Finally, he discusses a variant reading of 4:31 where 'kabir' (singular) is read instead of 'kabair' (plural), suggesting that the verse may refer specifically to disbelief as the great sin.

Key Terms
كبائر
معتزلة
صلاة
قبلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that committing sins, especially major ones, completely removes a person from faith.
Correction: Maturidi shows using Quran 4:137 that even repeated disbelief does not negate faith when it was present, so sins do not negate faith.

Checkpoint
What Quranic verse does Maturidi cite to argue that sin does not negate faith?
A) 4:31
B) 4:137
C) 6:160
D) 2:286

Key Takeaway
Maturidi uses Quran 4:137 to demonstrate that even after sin, faith was present, refuting the idea that sin negates faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Write down one verse or hadith you will hold on to when that fear strikes next.

Variant Reading of Quran 4:31 on Major Sins
Maturidi discusses a variant reading of Quran 4:31 with 'kabir' (singular).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the variant reading of Quran 4:31 where 'kabir' (singular) is read instead of 'kabair' (plural) and its significance in Maturidi's thought.

Core Idea
A variant reading clarifies that the 'great sin' (kabir) may refer to disbelief.

Imam Maturidi discusses a textual variant of Quran 4:31 where the word 'kabir' (singular) appears instead of 'kabair' (plural). This variant suggests that the verse may be specifically warning against a single great sin, which Maturidi hints could be disbelief.

Guided Path
1
Read the variant reading passage
Read the short Arabic passage about the variant reading and its translation. Notice that Maturidi mentions a transmitted reading with 'kabir'.
The standard reading is 'kabair' (plural).
2
Understand the textual issue
Recognize that the variant changes the meaning: 'the great sin' (singular) vs. 'the great sins' (plural).
Maturidi notes that it is permissible to intend a singular by a plural form.
3
Consider the implication
Maturidi's mention of this variant may indicate his view that the great sin being avoided is disbelief itself, aligning with his overall argument that sin does not negate faith unless it is disbelief.
This connects to the previous lesson's content.
4
Reflect on the key takeaway
The variant reading supports the idea that the major sin to avoid is disbelief, not all major sins in general.
This illustrates Maturidi's flexibility in interpreting Quranic readings.

intermediate

The Second Argument: Self-Knowledge of Sincerity
Maturidi presents the second argument that everyone knows his own sincerity, refuting the claimant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how self-knowledge of sincerity refutes the claim that a major sinner becomes a kafir.

Core Idea
Self-knowledge defeats the claim: no one lies about their own belief.

Maturidi presents a powerful second argument based on immediate self-knowledge. Every person knows whether they are sincere in their belief. If someone claims that a believer who commits a major sin becomes a disbeliever, then that claimant is effectively calling the believer a liar about his own belief. But the believer knows he is not lying, so the claimant becomes the liar.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise of self-knowledge
Recognize that every person has direct, certain knowledge of his own sincerity in belief. This is an undeniable fact.
This premise is used as a starting point for the argument.
2
Apply the premise to the claimant
The claimant who says a major sinner is a kafir is essentially calling the sinner a liar about his belief. Since the sinner knows he is sincere, the claimant becomes a liar before Allah and the believers.
The argument turns the tables on the claimant.
3
Grasp the scriptural support and conclusion
Maturidi cites the verse about those who believed then disbelieved then believed again. If a major sin made one a kafir, then reversion to faith would be impossible. The verse implies it is possible, so the claim is false.
This reinforces the logical conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني أن كلاً يعلم من نفسه في وقت اعتقاده أنه غير كاذب في ذلك، ثم يعلمه من في دينه. ولو كان به ظهور لكان لا علم يقع في الحقيقة. ولا قوة إلا بالله. بل مدعي يلزمه ذلك، إذ في اعتقاده أن لا يكذب. وكل مؤمن يعلم أنه بهذا القول كاذب، وكذلك الله سبحانه، إذ هو يعلم حقيقة كل شيء بما هو عليه لا بغيره، يعلم صدقه في الأول، وإن تعدى من بعده؛ فيصير صاحب هذا القول عند الله وعند من شهد عليه بالكذب كاذبًا، فيستوجب به من كل أحد القضاء عليه بالكفر في قوله الذي يريد به تثبيت كفر غيره. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وقوله تعالى: إن الذين آمنوا ثم كفروا ثم آمنوا ثم كفروا، ولو كان في ذلك ما ذكر لم يكن ليثبت لهم إيمان أبدا، فثبت به فساد قولهم. فهذا في الكفر، فكيف فيهما دونه. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
The second: that everyone knows from himself at the time of his belief that he is not lying in that, then knows it one who is in his religion. And if it were by appearance, then no knowledge would occur in reality. There is no power except with Allah. Rather the claimant is obliged by that, since in his belief he does not lie. And every believer knows that he is lying with this statement, and likewise Allah the Exalted, for He knows the reality of everything as it is not by other than it, He knows his truthfulness in the first, even if others follow after him; so the holder of this statement becomes before Allah and before those who testify against him with lying a liar, so he becomes deserving by everyone of the judgment of disbelief upon him in his statement by which he intends to establish the disbelief of others. There is no power except with Allah. And His saying the Exalted: 'Indeed those who believed then disbelieved then believed then disbelieved...' and if there were in that what was mentioned, their faith would never be established, so the corruption of their statement is established. So this regarding disbelief, so how about both without it? There is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage is a proof. It begins with the second argument that every person has immediate self-knowledge of his own sincerity in belief. If someone claims that a person who commits a major sin becomes a kafir, then that claimant is calling the sinner a liar in his belief. But the sinner knows he is not lying, so the claimant becomes a liar before Allah and the believers. The verse about repeated belief and disbelief supports this: if a major sin made one a kafir, then those who believed then disbelieved would never have faith re-established, but the verse implies they did. So the claim is false. Concludes that if this refutes the claim regarding disbelief, then it also refutes it for both belief and disbelief (i.e., for the intermediate state).

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Self-knowledge is subjective and cannot be used as evidence in theological arguments.
Correction: Maturidi uses self-knowledge as an undeniable premise because the opponent himself admits that a person knows his own sincerity. Thus it is a valid starting point for refutation.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's second argument, what happens to a person who claims that a believer who commits a major sin becomes a kafir?
A) He becomes truthful before Allah

D) He must provide evidence

Key Takeaway
Self-knowledge of sincerity is an undeniable premise that refutes the claim that major sin entails disbelief.

Application to Mu'tazila: Contradiction on Major Sinners
Maturidi applies the principle to Mu'tazila, highlighting their inconsistency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi exposes a logical contradiction in the Mu'tazilite position on major sinners.

Core Idea
What defines a name defines its loss — Maturidi's logical critique.

Imam Maturidi now applies a general principle about opposites and states to a specific case: the Mu'tazila. He finds that their affirmation of the name 'people of prayer' for major sinners, while denying them true faith, is logically inconsistent. This passage sharpens your ability to detect contradictions in theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic and translation together. Notice the concise structure: a general principle, an application, and a concluding wonder.
The passage is dense but clear.
2
Identify the contradiction
The Mu'tazila call major sinners 'people of prayer and qibla' but claim they lack full faith. Maturidi asks: if the name is based on faith, how can the name remain when faith is gone?
This highlights the inconsistency.
3
Reflect on the principle
Consider how the earlier principle about states and opposites supports this critique. The variation of states does not justify a contradiction between name and reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى ذلك اختلاف الأحوال في الخلق لا يوجب فساد مضاداتها في غير تلك الأحوال. وبالله التوفيق. وهذا أيضاً يلزم المعتزلة في الكبائر. ثم العجب من هؤلاء يثبتون لأصحاب الكبائر اسم أهل الصلاة والقبلة؛ وسبب إثبات هذا الاسم لهم الإيمان، فمحال زواله على بقاء ذلك، وما به ثبت قد زال. والله أعلم.
And upon that, the variation of states in creation does not necessitate the corruption of their opposites in other than those states. And with Allah is success. And this also applies to the Mu'tazila regarding major sins. Then the wonder from these: they affirm for the people of major sins the name of people of prayer and qibla; and the reason for affirming this name for them is faith, so it is impossible for it [faith] to vanish while that [name] remains, and that by which it is established has vanished. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage applies the previous principle to the Mu'tazila who affirm that major sinners are still called people of prayer and qibla, yet claim they are not believers. Maturidi points out that the reason for that name is faith, so if faith is gone, the name should also go. They are inconsistent.

Maturidi's Refutation of General Forgiveness Claim
Maturidi discusses variant reading and refutes sects' generality claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi uses a variant reading of a Qur'anic verse and contextual evidence to argue that the promise of forgiveness for avoiding major sins is specifically about avoiding disbelief and shirk, thereby refuting the claims of the Khawarij and Mu'tazila.

Core Idea
The verse on avoiding great sins actually refers to avoiding kufr and shirk, not all major sins.

This passage from Imam Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid addresses a key point of contention with the Khawarij and Mu'tazila. They argued that the verse 'If you avoid the great sins...' promises forgiveness for all sins if one avoids major sins. Maturidi shows through variant readings and other verses that the 'great sins' here are specifically disbelief and shirk, not all major sins, thus their claim of generality is invalid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation to grasp the flow of Maturidi's argument.
Notice the variant reading 'kabir' (singular) vs 'kaba'ir' (plural).
2
Identify the variant reading
Maturidi mentions a variant reading that uses the singular 'kabir' instead of plural 'kaba'ir'. Recognize that this opens the possibility of specific interpretation.
He does not reject the plural reading but uses the variant to show flexibility.
3
Examine the supporting verses
Maturidi cites verses about disbelief in faith, seeking other religions, and apostasy to show that the context is about kufr and shirk, not all major sins.
These verses all involve exiting from the fold of Islam.
4
Understand the refutation of sects
He shows that the Khawarij and Mu'tazila interpretations lead to admitting that the verse is specific to exiting faith, which contradicts their claim of generality.
Their own reasoning forces them to accept specificity.
5
Reflect on the principle
The passage concludes that the verse is specific, not general. This teaches us to interpret Qur'anic promises in light of other verses and variant readings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد روى في الآية القراءة على إن تجتنبوا كبير ما تنهون عنه، فإنه قد يجوز إرادة الآحاد بحرف الجمع، فلا ننكر أن تكون الآية على ذلك. يبين ذلك قوله تعالى: ومن يكفر بالإيمان الآية، وقوله: ومن يبتغ غير الإسلام دينا الآية، وقوله: ومن يرتدد منكم عن دينه الآية، وعلى ذلك تأويل قوله: إن الله لا يغفر أن يشرك به، ثم قال: ويغفر ما دون ذلك لمن يشاء؛ كما قال في هذا: ونكفر عنكم سيئاتكم، فيكون الإتيان بحكم واحد؛ ومعلوم أن لا درك للمعتزلة والخوارج في إحداهما فكذلك في الأخرى. ثم الأصل أن قوله: إن تجتنبوا كبائر ما تنهون عنه نكفر عنكم سيئاتكم، على قول الخوارج كأنه قال: إن تجتنبوا الكفر والشرك، وعلى قول المعتزلة: إن تجتنبوا الخروج من الإيمان نكفر عنكم ما ذكر، فلا كبيرة إذن على قولهم إلا الخروج من الإيمان؛ فصارت الآية على قولهم راجعة إلى خاص، وهو ما يخرج عن الدين والإيمان، فأبطل ذلك قولهم في دعوى العموم فيها، وألزم القول بالخصوص.
And it has been narrated regarding the verse the reading 'If you avoid great (singular) of what you are forbidden' ... then it may be that the singular is intended with a plural particle, so we do not deny that the verse is upon that. That is clarified by His saying: 'And whoever disbelieves in faith...' and His saying: 'And whoever seeks other than Islam as a religion...' and His saying: 'And whoever apostatizes from his religion...' and upon that is the interpretation of His saying: 'Indeed Allah does not forgive that partners be associated with Him, then He said: And He forgives what is below that for whom He wills'; as He said in this: 'And We will remove from you your misdeeds', so the bringing of one ruling becomes valid; and it is known that there is no perception for the Mu'tazila and Khawarij in one of them, so likewise in the other. Then the principle is that His saying: 'If you avoid the great sins of what you are forbidden, We will remove from you your misdeeds,' according to the statement of the Khawarij, it is as if He said: If you avoid disbelief and shirk, and according to the statement of the Mu'tazila: If you avoid exiting from faith, We will remove from you what is mentioned, so there is no major sin according to them except exiting from faith; so the verse according to them becomes specific to what expels from religion and faith, so that invalidates their claim of generality in it, and obligates the statement of specificity.
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage discusses the variant reading of the verse (singular 'kabir' vs plural 'kaba'ir') and argues that the subsequent verses show that the avoidance of major sins means avoidance of kufr and shirk, thus the promise of forgiveness is specific to that. This refutes the Khawarij and Mu'tazila who claim that the verse applies to all major sins. Maturidi shows that their interpretation leads to the verse being specific to exiting from faith, which contradicts their claim of generality.

Correction: Maturidi shows that the 'great sins' in this verse refer specifically to disbelief and shirk, not all major sins, based on variant reading and context.

The Logical Contradiction of Generalizing Promise and Threat Verses
Maturidi argues that generalizing both promise and threat verses leads to contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that interpreting both promise and threat verses as general leads to logical contradiction, and how this supports the need for specification.

Core Idea
If both promise and threat were general, the same person would be both rewarded and punished.

In this passage, Maturidi presents a powerful logical argument against taking all Quranic promises and threats as universal. He shows that doing so forces a contradiction onto a single individual, who cannot simultaneously deserve reward and punishment for the same action. This argument reveals the necessity of interpretation to resolve apparent conflicts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Maturidi sets up the problem: Allah promised good deeds and threatened evil deeds in general terms. If both are taken generally, a person can have both good and evil deeds.
Pay attention to the word 'عموم' (generality).
2
Identify the contradiction
Maturidi says that directing both to generality imposes contradiction when combining the two matters in one person. Think about why that is: the same person cannot be both promised paradise and threatened with hell simultaneously.
This is a logical, not textual, contradiction.
3
Understand the implication
Because of this contradiction, both cannot be general. At least one must be specific. Maturidi mentions that others (like al-Husayn) suspend judgment, while the Mu'tazila and Khawarij claim threat verses are general.
Maturidi's position leads to the need for specification, often through the condition of avoiding major sins.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فمن قضى بشيء دون شيء بلا بيان فهو متحكم. وفي ذلك لزوم قول الحسين من الوقف في جميع ما فيه الوعيد وبطلان قول من ذكر. والله أعلم. ثم الأصل أن الله وعد على كثير من الخيرات وعدا من غير ذكر اجتناب الكبائر معه وأوعد على كثير من السيئات وعيداً في مخرج العموم كما وعد على الخيرات. فمن وجه الآيتين جميعا إلى العموم ألزم التناقض في جمع الأمرين في واحد، وذلك آية السفه. ثم اضطربت في ذلك الأقاويل، فزعمت المعتزلة والخوارج أن آيات الوعيد أحق بالعموم
So whoever judges something without something without clarification is a dictator. And in that is the obligation of the statement of al-Husayn of stopping in all that contains threat, and the invalidation of the statement of those mentioned. And Allah knows best. Then the principle is that Allah promised upon many good deeds a promise without the mention of avoiding major sins with it, and threatened upon many evil deeds a threat in the form of generality just as He promised upon good deeds. So whoever directs both verses towards generality imposes contradiction in combining the two matters in one, and that is a sign of foolishness. Then the statements became confused in that, and the Mu'tazila and Khawarij claimed that the verses of threat are more deserving of generality.
Source pages p. 434

Commentary
This passage argues that if one interprets both the verses of promise and threat as general, then a single person could receive both promise and threat, which is contradictory. Therefore, they must be specific. The Mu'tazila and Khawarij claim that threat verses are general, but Maturidi says this leads to inconsistency. He also mentions the position of al-Husayn (possibly al-Karrabisi?) who suspends judgment on all threat verses.

Key Terms
وعد
وعيد
عموم
تناقض
وقف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Generalizing all Quranic verses about promise and threat is the safest approach because it takes them literally.
Correction: Maturidi shows that this leads to logical contradiction, so some interpretation (takhsis) is necessary to avoid conflict.

Checkpoint
What problem does Maturidi identify with interpreting both promise and threat verses as general?
A) They become contradictory for one person
B) They become abrogated
C) They become metaphorical
D) They lose meaning

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's logical argument shows that generalizing both promise and threat verses forces a contradiction, proving that at least one must be interpreted specifically.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were to take every promise of reward in the Qur'an as unconditional, how would you reconcile that with your own sins? Would you feel entitled to Paradise despite your disobedience? How does Maturidi's argument force you to reconsider your reliance on general promises?
?
The Mu'tazila and Khawarij prioritized threat verses because they wanted to uphold God's justice. But Maturidi shows this leads to confusion. In your own life, do you tend to focus more on God's punishment or His mercy? How does realizing both are conditional help you maintain a balanced fear and hope?
?
Maturidi says judging without full clarification is dictatorship. Reflect: In your understanding of Islam, do you ever insist on a literal interpretation without considering broader principles? Where might you be imposing a simplistic reading of scripture that leads to contradiction?

Faith Defined Through Quran and Hadith
Maturidi defines faith using Quranic verses and the hadith of Jibril.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi uses Quranic verses and the hadith of Jibril to define faith and establish that it is not nullified by sins.

Core Idea
Faith is defined by revelation, not diminished by sins.

Imam Maturidi grounds the definition of faith in primary sources. He cites Quranic verses that attach the name of faith to believers before they commit sins and a command not to accuse a Muslim of disbelief. He then references the hadith of Jibril, where the Prophet ﷺ defines the pillars of faith. This lesson unpacks how these texts establish a revelation-based understanding of faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get its flow. Then read the English translation. Notice the two Quranic references and the mention of Jibril's question.
The passage ends abruptly with 'فبين', referring to the well-known hadith of Jibril.
2
Identify the Quranic proofs
Find the two Quranic verses cited: one that declares believers (likely 2:136 or 2:285) and the prohibition in 4:94 against calling a believer an unbeliever. Understand how each supports the point.
Verse 4:94 is explicitly quoted: 'Do not say to one who gives you peace: You are not a believer.'
3
Reflect on the hadith reference
The passage alludes to the hadith of Jibril, where the Prophet defines faith (iman). Consider how this prophetic definition complements the Quranic evidence.
The full hadith lists belief in Allah, angels, books, messengers, the Last Day, and divine decree.
4
Apply the lesson
Ask yourself: What does this teach about judging others' faith? How does it counter the idea that sins negate faith?
The passage implies that as long as a person professes faith, we cannot declare them a disbeliever.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأصل في ذلك أن الله تعالى ألزمه اسم الإيمان قبل ارتكابه ما ارتكب وأزال عنه اسم الكفر بقوله: قولوا آمنا بالله الآية، وقوله: آمن الرسول الآية، فبين بما يكون المرء مؤمنا وحرم على من يقول لمثله: ألست مؤمنا بقوله: ولا تقولوا لمن ألقى إليكم السلام لست مؤمنا [النساء، ٩٤/٤]. وبين رسول الله حين سأله جبريل عن الإيمان، فبين
Then the principle in that is that Allah obligated for him the name of faith before his commission of what he committed and removed from him the name of disbelief by His saying: 'Say, 'We believe in Allah'' the verse, and His saying: 'The Messenger has believed' the verse. So He clarified by what a person becomes a believer and prohibited saying to someone like him: 'Are you not a believer?' by His saying: 'And do not say to one who gives you [the greeting of] peace, 'You are not a believer'' [4:94]. And the Messenger of Allah clarified when Jibril asked him about faith, so he clarified
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This passage is a definition of faith based on primary sources. The author argues that Allah has attached the name of faith to a person before they commit sins, and removed the name of disbelief from them, as shown in the verses 'Say, we believe in Allah...' (2:136) and 'The Messenger has believed...' (2:285). These verses establish the criteria for being a believer. Furthermore, Allah prohibits accusing others of not being believers, as in verse 4:94: 'Do not say to one who gives you peace: You are not a believer.' Finally, the Prophet Muhammad, upon being asked by Jibril (Gabriel) about faith, gave a definition. The text ends with 'so he clarified', implying the well-known hadith in which faith is defined as belief in Allah, His angels, His books, His messengers, and the Last Day, and belief in divine decree. The author uses these sources to show that faith is defined by revelation, not by human opinion, and that it is not lost by committing sins.

Key Terms
الإيمان
الكفر
الرسول
جبريل
السلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that committing major sins completely negates faith, making the sinner an unbeliever.
Correction: This passage shows that Allah still calls such a person a believer (as in the verses before sin) and prohibits calling them unbelievers (4:94). Thus, faith remains unless explicit disbelief occurs.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what does verse 4:94 prohibit?
A) Greeting non-Muslims
B) Calling a Muslim an unbeliever
C) Questioning faith
D) Abandoning prayer

Key Takeaway
Faith is divinely defined through Quran and Sunnah, and it remains with a believer even after sins unless they explicitly reject it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a fellow Muslim committing a sin, do you ever doubt their faith? What would the Prophet’s teaching about the one who gives the greeting of peace say to you in that moment? How will you change your response?
?
The Quran says 'Say, we believe in Allah' — do you truly recite that verse as a declaration of your own faith, or have these words become routine? What would it mean for you to own them fully today?
?
If your faith is not lost by your sins, then what is the real danger you face? How does this teaching make you more accountable for your actions, knowing that faith remains but deeds are judged?
