The Believer Defined by Obedience and the Status of Major Sinners
Maturidi defines believer by obedience and addresses the major sin controversy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's definition of a believer as one who obeys the divine command and his affirmation that the committer of a major sin remains a believer, contrary to the positions of the Mu'tazila and Khawarij.

Core Idea
A believer is verified by obedience, and major sin does not expel one from faith.

Maturidi begins by grounding the identity of a believer in the act of obedience to the divine command, referencing the famous hadith about fighting until people testify. He then turns to the contentious issue of major sinners, rejecting the extreme positions of the Mu'tazila and Khawarij and affirming that such a person remains a believer.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall flow. Notice the two main sections: defining the believer and addressing the major sin controversy.
The passage is compact, so read attentively.
2
Identify the definition of believer
Look for what action verifies the name 'believer'. Maturidi says it is 'doing that' — i.e., performing the commanded actions, likely the shahada and its implications.
He references the hadith 'I was commanded to fight people...' to emphasize the foundational act of testimony.
3
Understand the disagreement over major sinners
Note that Maturidi mentions the Mu'tazila and Khawarij. Recall that the Khawarij consider major sinners as disbelievers, while the Mu'tazila place them in a middle state. Maturidi criticizes both for their obstinacy and rebellion.
4
Grasp Maturidi's position
Maturidi affirms that the committer of a major sin is still a believer. He accuses the opposing sects of despairing of Allah's mercy and denying the possibility of divine pardon.
This reflects Maturidi's emphasis on hope and Allah's wisdom.
5
Reflect on the overall teaching
Consider how Maturidi balances the importance of deeds (obedience) with the core belief that faith is not nullified by sin. This is a key point in his theology.
The final phrase 'There is no power except with Allah' underscores reliance on God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذلك وحقق له اسم المؤمن بإتيان ذلك ; وكذلك أمرت أن أقاتل الناس إلى آخر ما ذكر. فهذا الآن مؤمن بالكتاب والسنة وما أجمعت عليه الأمة، وما شهدت له أهل اللغة . ثم اختلف في صاحب الكبيرة، لا يزال الذي ذلك وصفه بتعنت المعتزلة وتمرد الخوارج; بل يُدعى لهم بما آثروا لأنفسهم وأيسوا من رحمة الله، ولم يروا الله في حكمته جواز العفو عنهم. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Thus the name of believer is verified for him by doing that; and likewise I was commanded to fight people until the end of what was mentioned. So this one is now a believer according to the Book and the Sunna and what the community has agreed upon, and what the people of language have testified for him. Then there is disagreement concerning the committer of a major sin; he who is described with that still [is a believer?] due to the obstinacy of the Mu'tazila and the rebellion of the Khawarij; rather, they are called by what they chose for themselves and despaired of the mercy of Allah, and they did not see in Allah's wisdom the permissibility of pardoning them. And there is no power except with Allah.

Commentary
This passage defines the believer and addresses the controversy about the committer of major sins. It begins by stating that the name 'believer' is verified for a person by performing the commanded actions, referencing the hadith 'I was commanded to fight people until they say...' Then it asserts that such a person is a believer according to the Book, Sunna, consensus, and linguistic testimony. It then notes that disagreement arose regarding the committer of a major sin, and criticizes the Mu'tazila and Khawarij for their strict stance, accusing them of obstinacy and rebellion, and of despairing of Allah's mercy and denying the possibility of His pardon. The passage ends with the formula 'There is no power except with Allah.'

Correction: Maturidi teaches that the committer of a major sin remains a believer, though sinful, and may be pardoned by Allah's mercy.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi affirms that a person who obeys the command is a believer, and even committing a major sin does not strip one of that status; rather, the sinner remains a believer, contra the Mu'tazila and Khawarij.

Faith and Disbelief

Fisq, 'Isyan, and Zulm Are Not Contrary to Iman
Maturidi defines fisq, 'isyan, zulm and argues they do not negate iman.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that fisq, 'isyan, and zulm do not negate iman, and why Maturidi classifies departure from command into three types.

Core Idea
Sins do not remove faith; they are departures from command, not from belief.

Maturidi now turns to the relationship between sinful acts and faith. He clarifies that terms like fisq (disobedience), 'isyan (rebellion), and zulm (injustice) do not automatically negate iman. To understand this, he defines each term and shows that departure from command comes in different kinds—only one of which pertains to belief. This passage helps us see that not all sin is disbelief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the literal meaning.
Pay close attention to how each term is defined.
2
Understand the definitions
Notice that fisq is defined as 'departure from the command', zulm as 'placing a thing in other than its place', and 'isyan as 'opposition'.
3
Identify the three divisions of fisq
The passage divides departure from command into three: command of guidance, obligatory command, and command of belief.
This classification is key to the argument.
4
Reflect on the argument's conclusion
Maturidi warns that anyone who treats all departures as equivalent to remove the name of iman is wronging himself and refuting divine distinctions.
Consider why only departure from a command of belief would affect faith.
5
Connect to the broader lesson
This passage reinforces that faith is not nullified by sin unless the sin involves a departure from belief itself.
This is a central Maturidi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم المعاني التي هي للفسق والعصيان والظلم ليست بمضادة للإيمان، إذ الفسق اسم الخروج عن الأمر، وجارٍ ذلك على أقسام ثلاثة: يخرج مما هو أمر إرشاد، وفرض، واعتقاد; وكذلك الظلم، إذ هو اسم لوضع الشيء غير موضعه، والعصيان اسم للخلاف. فمن رتب الكل في الجزاء أو في حقيقة المعنى وأراد أن يزيل اسم الإيمان بكل ذلك فنفسه يظلم، ولدفع ما فرق الله ورسوله والأئمة يتعرض. ولا قوة إلا بالله . وقد تقدم بيان ما له اسم الإيمان.
Then the meanings that are for fisq, 'isyan, and zulm are not contrary to iman, for fisq is the name for departing from the command, and it runs on three divisions: departing from what is a command of guidance, an obligatory command, and a command of belief; and likewise zulm, for it is the name for placing a thing in other than its place, and 'isyan is the name for opposition. So whoever ranks all of them in recompense or in the reality of the meaning and wants to remove the name of iman by all of that, then he wrongs himself, and exposes himself to refuting what Allah and His Messenger and the imams have distinguished. And there is no power except with Allah. And the explanation of what the name of iman belongs to has preceded.
Source pages p. 436

Commentary
This passage is a classification and argument that the concepts of fisq (disobedience), 'isyan (rebellion), and zulm (injustice) do not contradict the reality of iman (faith). First, it defines fisq as 'departure from the command' and divides it into three types: departure from a command of guidance (irshad), an obligatory command (fard), and a command of belief (i'tiqad). Similarly, zulm is defined as placing something in other than its proper place, and 'isyan as opposition. The argument then warns that anyone who treats all these as equivalent in terms of punishment or essential meaning, and thereby attempts to remove the name of iman from a person, is guilty of self-injustice and is engaging in refuting the distinctions made by Allah, His Messenger, and the imams. The passage concludes with 'There is no power except with Allah' and notes that the explanation of what the name iman belongs to has already been given.

Key Terms
الفسق (fisq/disobedience)
العصيان ('isyan/rebellion)
الظلم (zulm/injustice)
أمر إرشاد (command of guidance)
أمر فرض (obligatory command)
أمر اعتقاد (command of belief)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any act of disobedience (fisq) removes a person from faith.
Correction: Maturidi explains that fisq applies to departure from commands of guidance, obligation, or belief; only departure from a command of belief affects iman.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, how many divisions of departure from command are there?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
Fisq, 'isyan, and zulm do not automatically negate iman; only departure from a command of belief affects faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever judged someone's faith based on a sin they committed? How does this lesson's distinction between commands challenge you to stop measuring others' iman by their actions?
?
When you sin, do you feel that your iman is completely lost? How can understanding that fisq is departure from command rather than from belief change your repentance and hope in Allah's mercy?
?
The lesson says that placing things in other than their place (zulm) does not negate iman. Does this truth make you more lenient with yourself or more serious about avoiding sin? What does it mean for your daily struggle with obedience?

Faith and Disbelief

Al-Ka'bi's Claim and Maturidi's Initial Refutation
Maturidi presents al-Ka'bi's view and his initial refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ka'bi's argument that the name 'mu'min' is not derived from action only, and Maturidi's initial refutation pointing out contradictions.

Core Idea
Faith is not merely a label; it has a real connection to action that al-Ka'bi's view distorts.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi presents and then critiques the view of al-Ka'bi, a prominent Mu'tazili theologian. Al-Ka'bi claims that the term 'mu'min' (believer) is not derived solely from action, nor is it just a mark, but a combination. Maturidi begins his refutation by examining al-Ka'bi's own words and exposing a fundamental inconsistency regarding the committer of major sins.

Guided Path
1
Understand al-Ka'bi's position
Read al-Ka'bi's definition carefully: he says 'mu'min' is not derived from action only, nor solely a mark, but a name derived from action, praise, and a mark for distinction.
Al-Ka'bi is trying to avoid both pure action-derivation and pure conventional labeling.
2
Notice Maturidi's method
Maturidi first clarifies what 'from iman' means before critiquing. He interprets 'every act of obedience is from iman' as meaning from the effect of iman, not that the act is itself iman.
This shows Maturidi's careful approach to language.
3
Identify the contradiction
Maturidi points out that al-Ka'bi admits the committer of a major sin has a praiseworthy action but refuses to call him a believer. This contradicts al-Ka'bi's own claim that the name is derived from action.
This is the first crack in al-Ka'bi's argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نذكر بعض الذي ذكره الكعبي لمذهبه من القول الذي اختاره وارتضاه ، ثم الحجاج له، بما يعلم من تأمّله مَبْلَغَه في دين الله . ولا قوة إلا بالله. فزعم أن قول أهل الحق: إن كل طاعة من الإيمان، واسم الإيمان يستحق بما يوجب تركه اسم الفسق قال وليس قولنا مؤمن بمشتق من الفعل فقط، لما لا يسمى به كل من صدق أحدًا وأطاعه وخضع له اسما مطلقا ; ولا هو أيضا سمة فقط، إذ لو كان سمة لجاز التسمية به لمن ليس هو كذلك، كما تسمى الحسناء قبيحة، وإذا لم يكن كذلك ثبت أنه اسم مشتق من فعل ومدح في الدين وسمة للتفرقة. قال الشيخ رحمه الله : نقول وبالله نستعين قوله : «أهل الحق كذا حق ، إن لم يرد بقوله : كل طاعة من الإيمان غير أنه من أثر الإيمان، وهو كما يقول: «كل نعمة من الله»، أي به نيلت وكانت وكذلك الإيمان . وقوله : (اسم الإيمان يستحق بكذا»، فقد نقضه حيث زعم أن مع صاحب الكبيرة فعلاً لكن لا يطلق له الاسم، ووصف في هذا ما ذكر، وذلك عنده في التحقيق اسم المؤمن لا اسم الإيمان ، إذ يحققه بدونه ولا يسميه به ، فهذا مبلغ علمه بقوله . وقوله : ليس باسم مشتق من فعله عجيب، إذ حقق لفعله ذلك الاسم ثم منع التسمية به، وذلك يوجب جواز تسمية كل أحد بغير اسم فعله في الحقيقة ومنع التسمية به; وفي ذلك لزوم اسم كل فاعل غير فاعل، وكل من ليس بفاعل فاعلاً، وكذا ذا في الحركة ونحو ذلك. وما قال من قوله: «من صدق كذا لا يسمى بذلك ، وأتبع ذلك قوله : «أجمع أهل الملة على تخطئة من فعل ذلك»، كذب من وجهين أحدهما أنه قال: «لا يطلق له الاسم»، بل هو مطلق له، لكن لا يُعرف ما يراد بذلك الإيمان، فلهذا يجب تبيينه لا لأن ذلك ليس باسم له. وكذلك لا يقال : «أطاع فلان» مطلقا بما أطاع أحداً من حيث لا يُعرف المراد، لا لأنه لم يستحق بفعله الإطلاق، ولكنه طاعة من لا يُعرف الأمر به ; وكذلك الإيمان، فإذا صار إلى المعرفة لزم القول به . وكذلك لا يقال : فلان مصدق أو مكذب بما كان منه ذلك في أحد حتى يتبين . ثم كل من كفر بالله يقال فيه: «مكذب»، لما عُرف حقيقته ، فمثله المؤمن. والله الموفق. وكذلك حكايته عن أهل الملة كذب. والعجب منه لا يزال يروى في هذا الكتاب عن الأمة في أشياء لعل وجوده عن واحد منهم يعسر ، فضلاً عن الأمة، ويجعل ذلك ذريعة لباطله، كأنه أمِنَ أن يتأمله من له لُبِّ أو أحد ممن ينازعه في المذهب. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وقوله : ليس بسمة له، فيقال له: لو لم يكن الاسم لتحقيق الفعل لم يكن ليمنع جعله
Then we mention some of what al-Ka'bi mentioned for his school, of the saying he chose and approved, then the argument against him, by which one who reflects knows his measure in the religion of Allah. And there is no power except with Allah. He claimed that the saying of the people of truth that every act of obedience is from iman, and the name of iman is deserved by that which necessitates the name of fisq if abandoned. He said: 'Our saying mu'min is not derived from the action only, because it is not applied as an absolute name to everyone who affirms someone, obeys him, and submits to him; nor is it only a mark, because if it were a mark it would be permissible to name with it one who is not such, just as a beautiful woman is called ugly, and if it is not such, then it is established that it is a name derived from an action and praise in the religion and a mark for distinction.' The Shaykh (may Allah have mercy on him) said: We say, and with Allah we seek help: His saying 'the people of truth is such and such is true, if he did not mean by his saying: every act of obedience is from iman, except that it is from the effect of iman, and it is like his saying: 'Every blessing is from Allah', i.e., by it it is attained and was, and likewise iman. And his saying: 'the name of iman is deserved by such and such' – he has contradicted it in that he claimed that with the committer of a major sin there is an action, but he does not apply the name to him, and he described in this what he mentioned, and that according to him in reality is the name of mu'min, not the name of iman, for he verifies it without it and does not call him by it. So this is the extent of his knowledge with his statement. And his saying: 'it is not a name derived from his action' is strange, for he verified that his action has that name then prevented naming by it, and that necessitates the permissibility of naming everyone by other than his actual name in reality and prevention of naming by it; and in that there is the necessary consequence that every doer is not a doer, and everyone who is not a doer is a doer, and likewise in motion and so on. And what he said in his statement: 'whoever affirms such and such is not called by that', and he followed that with his statement: 'the people of the religion are agreed on deeming mistaken the one who does that', is a lie in two respects: one of them is that he said: 'the name is not applied to him', rather it is applied to him, but what is intended by that iman is not known, so for this reason it must be clarified, not because it is not a name for him. And likewise it is not said 'so-and-so obeyed' absolutely because he obeyed someone, from the standpoint that the object is not known, not because he did not deserve the absolute term by his action, but it is an obedience of one whose command is not known; likewise iman, so when it comes to knowledge, the statement [about him] is necessary. And likewise it is not said: 'so-and-so is a verifier or a denier' because of what was from him in that in someone until it is clarified. Then everyone who disbelieves in Allah is said [about him]: 'denier', because his reality is known, so similarly the believer. And Allah is the Granter of success. Likewise his narration from the people of the religion is a lie. And it is strange from him that he continues to relate in this book from the community things whose existence from even one of them is difficult, let alone from the community, and he makes that a pretext for his falsehood, as if he is secure that no one with intellect or one of those who dispute with him in the school will reflect on it. And there is no power except with Allah. And his saying: 'it is not a mark for him' – so it is said to him: if the name were not for the verification of the action, it would not be to prevent making it...
Source pages p. 436

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer: Maturidi presents al-Ka'bi's view and then refutes it. Al-Ka'bi argues that the name 'mu'min' is not derived from action only, nor solely a mark, but a combination. The Shaykh's response begins by analyzing al-Ka'bi's words, pointing out contradictions: al-Ka'bi admits that the committer of a major sin has a praiseworthy action but denies him the name 'mu'min'. Then he attacks al-Ka'bi's denial that the name is derived from action, showing that it leads to absurdities like calling a doer a non-doer. He also refutes al-Ka'bi's claim of consensus, calling it a lie, and clarifies that the name is applied but needs context. The argument uses linguistic examples and logical consequences to defend that 'mu'min' is indeed derived from action. The passage ends with a final rebuttal about the name being a mark.

Key Terms
الكعبي (al-Ka'bi)
اسم مشتق (derived name)
سمة (mark)
أهل الحق (people of truth)
تخطئة (declaring mistaken)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That faith is just a label independent of actions
Correction: Maturidi shows that even al-Ka'bi admits actions are involved but inconsistently denies the name to the major sinner, proving that faith is tied to action.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of al-Ka'bi's claim that Maturidi refutes in this passage?
A) That faith is only a mark
B) That faith is derived from action only
C) That the name 'mu'min' is not derived from action only but is a combination of derivation and mark
D) That there is no name for faith

Key Takeaway
Al-Ka'bi's view is self-contradictory because he admits actions matter for faith but refuses to name the major sinner as believer.

Faith and Disbelief
intermediate

Maturidi's Further Refutation: Derived Name and Consensus
Maturidi refutes al-Ka'bi's claim about derived name, consensus, and marks

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's arguments against al-Ka'bi's denial that 'mu'min' is derived from action, and his refutation of the claimed consensus.

Core Idea
Denying that 'mu'min' is derived from action leads to absurdity and contradicts the practice of the community.

Maturidi continues his refutation by attacking al-Ka'bi's claim that the name is not derived from action. He shows that this leads to logical absurdities, such as calling a doer a non-doer. He also refutes al-Ka'bi's appeal to consensus, calling it a lie, and clarifies that the name is applied but requires context. Finally, he addresses al-Ka'bi's point about the name being a mark.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation of derived name
Maturidi finds it strange that al-Ka'bi verifies that the action has a name but denies naming by it. This leads to absurdity: a doer cannot be called a doer.
This is a logical consequence argument.
2
Understand the logical consequence
Maturidi points out that if we accept al-Ka'bi's position, we would have to call every doer a non-doer and vice versa, which is contradictory.
This shows the necessity of deriving names from actions.
3
Analyze the consensus refutation
Maturidi denies al-Ka'bi's claim of consensus, calling it a lie on two counts: the name is applied but needs clarification, and the consensus itself is fabricated.
Maturidi gives examples of obedience and tasdiq to illustrate.
4
See the closing argument about marks
Maturidi argues that if the name were not for verifying action, it would not prevent making it a mere mark. This supports the connection between name and action.
The passage ends mid-sentence, but the point is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نذكر بعض الذي ذكره الكعبي لمذهبه من القول الذي اختاره وارتضاه ، ثم الحجاج له، بما يعلم من تأمّله مَبْلَغَه في دين الله . ولا قوة إلا بالله. فزعم أن قول أهل الحق: إن كل طاعة من الإيمان، واسم الإيمان يستحق بما يوجب تركه اسم الفسق قال وليس قولنا مؤمن بمشتق من الفعل فقط، لما لا يسمى به كل من صدق أحدًا وأطاعه وخضع له اسما مطلقا ; ولا هو أيضا سمة فقط، إذ لو كان سمة لجاز التسمية به لمن ليس هو كذلك، كما تسمى الحسناء قبيحة، وإذا لم يكن كذلك ثبت أنه اسم مشتق من فعل ومدح في الدين وسمة للتفرقة. قال الشيخ رحمه الله : نقول وبالله نستعين قوله : «أهل الحق كذا حق ، إن لم يرد بقوله : كل طاعة من الإيمان غير أنه من أثر الإيمان، وهو كما يقول: «كل نعمة من الله»، أي به نيلت وكانت وكذلك الإيمان . وقوله : (اسم الإيمان يستحق بكذا»، فقد نقضه حيث زعم أن مع صاحب الكبيرة فعلاً لكن لا يطلق له الاسم، ووصف في هذا ما ذكر، وذلك عنده في التحقيق اسم المؤمن لا اسم الإيمان ، إذ يحققه بدونه ولا يسميه به ، فهذا مبلغ علمه بقوله . وقوله : ليس باسم مشتق من فعله عجيب، إذ حقق لفعله ذلك الاسم ثم منع التسمية به، وذلك يوجب جواز تسمية كل أحد بغير اسم فعله في الحقيقة ومنع التسمية به; وفي ذلك لزوم اسم كل فاعل غير فاعل، وكل من ليس بفاعل فاعلاً، وكذا ذا في الحركة ونحو ذلك. وما قال من قوله: «من صدق كذا لا يسمى بذلك ، وأتبع ذلك قوله : «أجمع أهل الملة على تخطئة من فعل ذلك»، كذب من وجهين أحدهما أنه قال: «لا يطلق له الاسم»، بل هو مطلق له، لكن لا يُعرف ما يراد بذلك الإيمان، فلهذا يجب تبيينه لا لأن ذلك ليس باسم له. وكذلك لا يقال : «أطاع فلان» مطلقا بما أطاع أحداً من حيث لا يُعرف المراد، لا لأنه لم يستحق بفعله الإطلاق، ولكنه طاعة من لا يُعرف الأمر به ; وكذلك الإيمان، فإذا صار إلى المعرفة لزم القول به . وكذلك لا يقال : فلان مصدق أو مكذب بما كان منه ذلك في أحد حتى يتبين . ثم كل من كفر بالله يقال فيه: «مكذب»، لما عُرف حقيقته ، فمثله المؤمن. والله الموفق. وكذلك حكايته عن أهل الملة كذب. والعجب منه لا يزال يروى في هذا الكتاب عن الأمة في أشياء لعل وجوده عن واحد منهم يعسر ، فضلاً عن الأمة، ويجعل ذلك ذريعة لباطله، كأنه أمِنَ أن يتأمله من له لُبِّ أو أحد ممن ينازعه في المذهب. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وقوله : ليس بسمة له، فيقال له: لو لم يكن الاسم لتحقيق الفعل لم يكن ليمنع جعله
Then we mention some of what al-Ka'bi mentioned for his school, of the saying he chose and approved, then the argument against him, by which one who reflects knows his measure in the religion of Allah. And there is no power except with Allah. He claimed that the saying of the people of truth that every act of obedience is from iman, and the name of iman is deserved by that which necessitates the name of fisq if abandoned. He said: 'Our saying mu'min is not derived from the action only, because it is not applied as an absolute name to everyone who affirms someone, obeys him, and submits to him; nor is it only a mark, because if it were a mark it would be permissible to name with it one who is not such, just as a beautiful woman is called ugly, and if it is not such, then it is established that it is a name derived from an action and praise in the religion and a mark for distinction.' The Shaykh (may Allah have mercy on him) said: We say, and with Allah we seek help: His saying 'the people of truth is such and such is true, if he did not mean by his saying: every act of obedience is from iman, except that it is from the effect of iman, and it is like his saying: 'Every blessing is from Allah', i.e., by it it is attained and was, and likewise iman. And his saying: 'the name of iman is deserved by such and such' – he has contradicted it in that he claimed that with the committer of a major sin there is an action, but he does not apply the name to him, and he described in this what he mentioned, and that according to him in reality is the name of mu'min, not the name of iman, for he verifies it without it and does not call him by it. So this is the extent of his knowledge with his statement. And his saying: 'it is not a name derived from his action' is strange, for he verified that his action has that name then prevented naming by it, and that necessitates the permissibility of naming everyone by other than his actual name in reality and prevention of naming by it; and in that there is the necessary consequence that every doer is not a doer, and everyone who is not a doer is a doer, and likewise in motion and so on. And what he said in his statement: 'whoever affirms such and such is not called by that', and he followed that with his statement: 'the people of the religion are agreed on deeming mistaken the one who does that', is a lie in two respects: one of them is that he said: 'the name is not applied to him', rather it is applied to him, but what is intended by that iman is not known, so for this reason it must be clarified, not because it is not a name for him. And likewise it is not said 'so-and-so obeyed' absolutely because he obeyed someone, from the standpoint that the object is not known, not because he did not deserve the absolute term by his action, but it is an obedience of one whose command is not known; likewise iman, so when it comes to knowledge, the statement [about him] is necessary. And likewise it is not said: 'so-and-so is a verifier or a denier' because of what was from him in that in someone until it is clarified. Then everyone who disbelieves in Allah is said [about him]: 'denier', because his reality is known, so similarly the believer. And Allah is the Granter of success. Likewise his narration from the people of the religion is a lie. And it is strange from him that he continues to relate in this book from the community things whose existence from even one of them is difficult, let alone from the community, and he makes that a pretext for his falsehood, as if he is secure that no one with intellect or one of those who dispute with him in the school will reflect on it. And there is no power except with Allah. And his saying: 'it is not a mark for him' – so it is said to him: if the name were not for the verification of the action, it would not be to prevent making it...
Source pages p. 436

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer: Maturidi presents al-Ka'bi's view and then refutes it. Al-Ka'bi argues that the name 'mu'min' is not derived from action only, nor solely a mark, but a combination. The Shaykh's response begins by analyzing al-Ka'bi's words, pointing out contradictions: al-Ka'bi admits that the committer of a major sin has a praiseworthy action but denies him the name 'mu'min'. Then he attacks al-Ka'bi's denial that the name is derived from action, showing that it leads to absurdities like calling a doer a non-doer. He also refutes al-Ka'bi's claim of consensus, calling it a lie, and clarifies that the name is applied but needs context. The argument uses linguistic examples and logical consequences to defend that 'mu'min' is indeed derived from action. The passage ends with a final rebuttal about the name being a mark.

اسم مشتق (derived name)
سمة (mark)
أهل الحق (people of truth)
تخطئة (declaring mistaken)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That there is no connection between the name 'mu'min' and action
Correction: Maturidi shows that denying the derivation leads to logical contradictions and that the community's practice confirms the connection.

Key Takeaway

Faith and Acts: A Logical Refutation
Maturidi refutes the opponent's view on faith and acts using logical arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's logical refutation of the claim that acts are not part of faith and why denying the derivative name leads to absurdity.

Core Idea
Faith and acts are inseparable; denying the name from the act leads to logical chaos.

Maturidi presents a tight logical refutation of an opponent who claims that acts of obedience are merely effects of faith, not part of it. The opponent also says that the name 'faith' is not derived from the act, leading to contradictions. In this passage, Maturidi exposes these inconsistencies and demonstrates that the opponent's position leads to absurd consequences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the opponent's claims
Read the Arabic and translation to see the opponent's statements that Maturidi refutes. Notice how Maturidi first quotes the opponent.
The opponent's view is embedded within Maturidi's response.
2
Follow Maturidi's first refutation: the blessing analogy
The opponent says acts are from faith as an effect, not essence. Maturidi compares this to saying blessings are from God—meaning they are attained through Him. See how this analogy weakens the opponent's distinction.
3
Analyze the second refutation: the name derivation absurdity
The opponent says 'it is not a name derived from his act.' Maturidi shows this leads to calling agents non-agents. Understand the logical problem.
This is the core of the refutation.
4
Examine the final point: consensus claim and clarification
The opponent claims consensus that one who does such is mistaken. Maturidi calls it a lie and explains that the name is applied but unknown; clarification is needed. See how he ends with the affirmation of naming for believers just as for disbelievers.
The passage ends with a strong analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ رحمه الله : نقول وبالله نستعين ; قوله : «أهل الحق كذا حق ، إن لم يرد بقوله : كل طاعة من الإيمان غير أنه من أثر الإيمان، وهو كما يقول: «كل نعمة من الله»، أي به نيلت وكانت وكذلك الإيمان . وقوله : (اسم الإيمان يستحق بكذا»، فقد نقضه حيث زعم أن مع صاحب الكبيرة فعلاً لكن لا يطلق له الاسم، ووصف في هذا ما ذكر، وذلك عنده في التحقيق اسم المؤمن لا اسم الإيمان ، إذ يحققه بدونه ولا يسميه به ، فهذا مبلغ علمه بقوله . وقوله : ليس باسم مشتق من فعله عجيب، إذ حقق لفعله ذلك الاسم ثم منع التسمية به، وذلك يوجب جواز تسمية كل أحد بغير اسم فعله في الحقيقة ومنع التسمية به؛ وفي ذلك لزوم اسم كل فاعل غير فاعل، وكل من ليس بفاعل فاعلاً، وكذا ذا في الحركة ونحو ذلك. وما قال من قوله: «من صدق كذا لا يسمى بذلك ، وأتبع ذلك قوله : «أجمع أهل الملة على تخطئة من فعل ذلك»، كذب من وجهين أحدهما أنه قال: «لا يطلق له الاسم»، بل هو مطلق له، لكن لا يُعرف ما يراد بذلك الإيمان، فلهذا يجب تبيينه لا لأن ذلك ليس باسم له. وكذلك لا يقال : «أطاع فلان» مطلقا بما أطاع أحداً من حيث لا يُعرف المراد، لا لأنه لم يستحق بفعله الإطلاق، ولكنه طاعة من لا يُعرف الأمر به ; وكذلك الإيمان، فإذا صار إلى المعرفة لزم القول به . وكذلك لا يقال : فلان مصدق أو مكذب بمعنى ما كان منه ذلك في أحد حتى يتبين . ثم كل من كفر بالله يقال فيه: «مكذب»، لما عُرف حقيقته ، فمثله المؤمن. والله الموفق.
The Shaykh, may God have mercy on him, said: We say, and with God we seek help. His statement: 'The people of truth are thus true, if he did not intend by his statement: every act of obedience is from faith, except that it is from the effect of faith.' And it is as he says: 'Every blessing is from God,' meaning by it it was attained and existed, and similarly faith. And his statement: 'The name of faith is deserved by such-and-such,' then he contradicted it when he claimed that the possessor of a major sin has an act but the name is not applied to him, and he described in this what he mentioned. And that according to him in reality is the name of 'believer' (mu'min), not the name of faith (iman), since he affirms it without it and does not call him by it. This is the extent of his knowledge of his statement. And his statement: 'It is not a name derived from his act' is strange, since he affirmed for his act that name and then forbade naming by it; and that necessitates the permissibility of naming everyone by other than the name of his act in reality and forbidding naming by it; and in that is the necessary attribution of the name of every agent as non-agent, and of everyone who is not an agent as an agent, and likewise in motion and the like. And what he said in his statement: 'Whoever affirms such-and-such is not called by that,' and he followed that with his statement: 'The people of the religion are agreed on declaring mistaken whoever does that,' is a lie in two respects: one of them is that he said: 'The name is not applied to him,' but rather it is applied to him, but what is intended by that faith is not known; therefore it must be clarified, not because that is not a name for him. Similarly, it is not said: 'So-and-so obeyed' absolutely, with obedience to someone, since the intended meaning is not known, not because he did not deserve absoluteness by his act, but it is obedience of one whose command is not known; and similarly faith; when it comes to knowledge, the statement [of it] becomes obligatory. Similarly, it is not said: 'So-and-so is a verifier or falsifier' regarding what was from him in something until it is clarified. Then everyone who disbelieves in God is said about him: 'Falsifier' (mukadhdhib), since his reality is known; likewise the believer. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 437

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an opponent's view on the relationship between acts of obedience and faith. The opponent claims that every act of obedience is from faith but only as an effect, not as the essence. Maturidi begins by comparing this to the statement that every blessing is from God, meaning it is attained through Him. He then points out a contradiction: the opponent says the name of faith is deserved by an act but then denies calling the possessor of a major sin a believer, even though he has an act. The opponent distinguishes between the name 'believer' (mu'min) and 'faith' (iman), but Maturidi finds this inconsistent. Next, he attacks the claim that 'it is not a name derived from his act' as absurd, because if the act merits the name, then forbidding it leads to logical problems: it would allow calling someone by a name not based on his actual act, leading to contradictions in agency attribution. He then accuses the opponent of lying when claiming consensus that one who does such is mistaken. The opponent's error is in saying the name is not applied when in fact it is, but the meaning is unknown; clarification is needed, not denial. Maturidi analogizes to obedience: we don't say 'so-and-so obeyed' absolutely because the command is unknown, but once known, we affirm. Similarly, we don't call someone a verifier or falsifier until the matter is clear. However, for disbelief, we readily call a disbeliever a 'falsifier' because the reality is known; likewise, a believer should be called a believer. The passage concludes with 'And God is the Grantor of success.'

Key Terms
إيمان
طاعة
اسم
مشتق
مؤمن
كافر
مكذب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Acts of obedience are not part of faith and the name 'faith' is not derived from the act of obedience.
Correction: Maturidi shows that if the act merits the name (faith), denying the name leads to logical absurdity, so the act is indeed connected to faith and naming is based on the act.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the problem with the opponent's claim that the name of faith is not derived from the act?
A) It contradicts the consensus of the scholars.
B) It necessitates the absurdity of calling an agent a non-agent and vice versa.
C) It makes faith impossible to define.
D) It implies that God is unjust.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that denying the name of faith to the believer on the basis of the act leads to logical contradictions, affirming that faith and acts are intrinsically connected.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a major sin, do you immediately doubt whether you are still a believer? How does Maturidi's argument that the name 'believer' is derived from your act of faith challenge you to see your sins as contradictions within your faith rather than negations of it? What practical step can you take this week to align your actions more closely with your belief?
?
Have you ever separated your outward actions from your inner faith, thinking that obedience is optional for salvation? This passage calls that view logically absurd. How will you change one daily habit to make your faith visible in your actions?
?
The opponent in this passage tried to distinguish between having faith and being called a believer. In your own life, are you ever reluctant to openly identify as a 'mu'min' because of your shortcomings? What would it take for you to fully embrace the name 'believer' as derived from your acts of worship and obedience, despite your flaws?

The Naming of the Grave Sinner: A Logical Trichotomy
Maturidi forces a logical trichotomy on the opponent's naming of the fasiq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's logical argument that refusing to call a grave sinner a believer while not denying his belief is inconsistent, and the correct position that the name follows the reality.

Core Idea
Withholding the name 'believer' from a sinner contradicts affirming his belief.

Maturidi engages with an opponent who tries to avoid calling a grave sinner a believer without outright denying his faith. This lesson examines Maturidi's incisive response, which forces a clear logical choice between three possibilities. The passage sharpens our understanding of how names derived from acts relate to reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's position
Read the passage where the opponent says: 'We do not say the sinner is not a believer, but we do not call him by it.' Notice the ambiguity.
2
Identify the three options
Maturidi presents a trichotomy: either the sinner is in reality a believer, not a believer, or neither. Write down each option.
3
Analyze the first option
If the opponent admits the sinner is a believer (first option), then refusing to call him a believer is contradictory. Maturidi says such a person is worse than a blind follower.
4
Analyze the other options
If the opponent chooses the other two options, he contradicts himself because he earlier claimed not to call him a believer but also not to deny his belief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك حكايته عن حكايته عن أهل الملة كذب. والعجب منه لا يزال يروى في هذا الكتاب عن الأمة في أشياء لعل وجوده عن واحد منهم يعسر، فضلاً عن الأمة، ويجعل ذلك ذريعة لباطله، كأنه أمن أن يتأمله من له لب أو أحد ممن ينازعه في المذهب. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وقوله : ليس بسمة له، فيقال له: لو لم يكن الاسم لتحقيق الفعل لم يكن ليمنع جعله سمة، لكنه في جعله تلبيس أنه سمي به بحق السمة، أو بحق حقيقة الفعل. وكذلك جميع ما عليه أمر الأسماء المشتقة عن الأفعال أن لم تجعل سمة لمن ليس له حقيقة إلا على المجاز والهزوء به. ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم قال : لا نقول في الفاسق عند التحقيق: إنه ليس بمؤمن، بل لا نسميه به. فيقال له : هو في التحقيق مؤمن، أو ليس بمؤمن، أو لا مؤمن ولا كافر؟ فإن قال بالأول فهو رجل دعته نفسه إلى تكذيبها فيما هي ليست بكاذبة فأطاعها، فحق مثله الإغضاء عنه، لأنه دون كل مقلد. فإن قال بالوجهين الآخرين فقد أكذب نفسه فيها حكى عنها. والله الموفق. ثم استدل على تسمية جميع الطاعات إيمانا بما هي من الدين عند الجميع، وبقوله: ﴿ومن يبتغ غير الإسلام دينا﴾ الآية، وقد بينا بعد من تعلق بالآية عن حقيقتها. مع ما لا ريب أن مبتغى شيء من العبادات على الإشارة إليها بلا اعتقاد الإسلام دينا غير مقبول منه، وإنما يقبل كل عبادة بدين الإسلام. ثبت أنها اسم عبادة مشار إليها، بها يقبل كل عبادة ولقوتها يرد. وهذا معنى قول الجميع من الدين ولا كل شيء يضاف إلى شيء بأنه منه يجب له اسمه. قال الله تعالى: ﴿وما بكم من نعمة فمن الله﴾، لا أنها هي هو. بل في ذلك دليل على غيرية الدين حتى يضاف إليه، فأقام به بعد الفراغ منه. والله الموفق. ثم احتج بقوله: ﴿وبشر المؤمنين بأن لهم من الله فضلا كبيرا﴾، ونحو ذلك من الوعد للمؤمنين باسم المطلق، ثم لم يكن ذلك لمرتكب الفسق فيه، وقد أجمع على عموم الوعد وإن اختلف في عموم الوعيد.
And similarly, his narration from his narration from the people of the religion is a lie. And it is astonishing that he continues to narrate in this book about the community (umma) things whose existence from a single one of them is difficult, let alone from the community, and he makes that a pretext for his falsehood, as if he feels secure that no one with intellect or anyone who disputes his school will examine it. And there is no power except with God. And his statement: 'It is not a mark (sima) for him,' so it is said to him: If the name were not for the affirmation of the act, it would not prevent making it a mark, but in his making it there is obfuscation: whether it is called by it by right of the mark or by right of the reality of the act. And similarly, the whole matter of names derived from acts is that they are not made a mark for one who has no reality except metaphorically and mockingly. And there is no power except with God. Then he said: 'We do not say about the sinner (fasiq) in reality: He is not a believer, but we do not call him by it.' So it is said to him: Is he in reality a believer, or not a believer, or neither a believer nor a disbeliever? If he says the first, he is a man whose soul called him to deny it regarding what is not false, so he obeyed it, and the like of him deserves to be overlooked, because he is below every imitator (muqallid). If he says the other two, he has contradicted himself in what he narrated about it. And God is the Grantor of success. Then he adduced as evidence for calling all acts of obedience 'iman' (faith) because they are part of the religion according to all, and by His statement: 'And whoever seeks a religion other than Islam...' the verse, and we have already explained after who clings to the verse regarding its reality. Along with the fact that there is no doubt that one who seeks something of acts of worship by indicating them without believing Islam as a religion is not accepted from him, but every act of worship is accepted only with the religion of Islam. It is established that it is a name for worship indicated, by which every act of worship is accepted and for its lack it is rejected. And this is the meaning of the statement of all: from the religion. And not everything added to something as being from it necessitates its name. God Almighty said: 'And whatever blessing you have is from God,' not that it is He. Rather in that is evidence of the otherness of the religion until it is added to it, and he established it after being free from it. And God is the Grantor of success. Then he argued by His statement: 'And give glad tidings to the believers that they will have from God great bounty,' and similar promises to believers by the absolute name, and that was not for the committer of debauchery (fisq) therein, and they have agreed on the generality of the promise although they differed on the generality of the threat.
Source pages p. 438

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of the refutation and a new argument about naming the grave sinner (fasiq) and proving that all acts of obedience are called iman. Maturidi first dismisses the opponent's narration about 'the people of the religion' as a lie and criticizes his habit of citing the community for things that have no basis, using it as a pretext for falsehood. He then addresses the opponent's statement that the name is not a 'mark' (sima) for the person. Maturidi argues that if the name were not for affirming the act, it would not be prevented from being a mark, but the opponent's making it a mark is obfuscation. The opponent's position leads to a false choice: either the name is by right of the mark (i.e., a mere label) or by right of the reality of the act. Maturidi states that derived names are not used as marks for someone who has no reality except metaphorically. Next, the opponent says about the sinner: 'We do not say he is not a believer in reality, but we do not call him by it.' Maturidi responds by forcing a trichotomy: is the sinner in reality a believer, not a believer, or neither? If the opponent says he is a believer, then he is worse than every blind follower (muqallid) because he obeys his own soul's denial of the truth. If he says the other two, he contradicts himself. Then Maturidi provides a proof that all acts of obedience are called iman because they are from the religion, citing the Quranic verse 'And whoever seeks a religion other than Islam...' (Quran 3:85). He explains that any act of worship without belief in Islam as religion is not accepted, so the acts are associated with the religion. He clarifies that adding something to something does not make it identical, as in the verse 'And whatever blessing you have is from God' (Quran 16:53) — the blessing is not God Himself. Similarly, acts are from religion but not identical to it. Finally, he mentions the verse 'And give glad tidings to the believers...' (Quran 33:47) to show that the promise is for believers absolutely, not for those who commit major sins, and there is consensus on the generality of the promise but disagreement on the generality of the threat.

Key Terms
فاسق
مؤمن
كافر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that refusing to call a grave sinner a 'believer' is a neutral position that avoids calling him a disbeliever.
Correction: Maturidi shows that if the sinner is truly a believer in reality, withholding the name is logically inconsistent and morally blameworthy.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the logical problem with the opponent's position?
A) It contradicts the Quranic verse about glad tidings.
B) It forces a trichotomy that leads to contradiction or worse.
C) It denies that acts of obedience are part of faith.
D) It is the position of the majority of scholars.

Key Takeaway
A name derived from an act must be applied based on the reality of that act; to affirm the reality but withhold the name is logically inconsistent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever withhold the name 'believer' from someone whose faith you actually affirm, based on their actions? How does Maturidi's argument expose your inconsistency, and what will you do to align your naming with reality?
?
When you look at your own life, are there truths you affirm in your heart but refuse to speak or act upon? What specific truth are you withholding from yourself or others, and what step will you take today to end that contradiction?

The Quranic Proof That All Acts Are Called Faith
Maturidi proves all acts of obedience are called iman using Quran and semantic principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's proof from the Quran that all acts of obedience are named iman because they are part of the religion, and the distinction between something being from something and being identical to it.

Core Idea
Acts are called iman because they belong to religion, not because they are religion itself.

Having addressed the naming of the sinner, Maturidi turns to a foundational proof that all acts of obedience are rightfully called iman. He cites a key Quranic verse and clarifies a crucial semantic principle: being 'from' something does not mean being identical to it. This lesson unpacks that argument and its supporting evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof and the Quranic verse
Read the passage where Maturidi states that acts of obedience are called iman because they are from the religion, citing Quran 3:85.
2
Understand the principle of 'being from'
Maturidi explains that something being 'from' another does not mean it is identical. He uses Quran 16:53 as an example: blessings are from God, not God Himself.
3
See the additional evidence
Maturidi then cites Quran 33:47, where believers are promised bounty, but this promise is not for those who commit fisq, showing that the name 'believer' applies to those who obey.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how these verses together establish that all acts of obedience are included in the name iman, and that iman is not merely inner belief but includes acts.

And similarly, his narration from his narration from the people of the religion is a lie. The Status of the Fasiq: A Dialectical Trap
Maturidi presents a logical trichotomy to refute an inconsistent position on the fasiq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's logical refutation of those who refuse to call the fasiq a believer or a disbeliever, and why that position is self-contradictory.

Core Idea
Not naming the fasiq a believer leads to a logical contradiction.

Maturidi presents a brief but sharp dialectical argument against a specific position regarding the fasiq (grave sinner). The opponent claims that we should neither call the fasiq a believer nor deny his faith outright. Maturidi forces the opponent into a trichotomy—believer, not believer, or neither—and shows that each option leads to self-contradiction. This passage reveals the precision of kalam logic in defending orthodox positions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the dialectical structure: the opponent's claim is presented, then a three-part question is posed.
2
Identify the three possible answers
The passage presents three logical possibilities: the fasiq is a believer, not a believer, or neither. See how Maturidi responds to each option.
3
Understand the consequences of each answer
If the opponent says the fasiq is a believer, he contradicts his own statement that he is not named a believer. If he says either of the other two, he contradicts himself in what he reported from himself. The first type is considered worse than a blind follower.
The term 'mukallid' (blind follower) implies someone who follows without evidence, which is a lower level of knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال : لا نقول في الفاسق عند التحقيق: إنه ليس بمؤمن»، بل لا نسميه به. فيقال له : هو في التحقيق مؤمن، أو ليس بمؤمن، أو لا مؤمن ولا كافر؟ فإن قال بالأول فهو رجل دعته نفسه إلى تكذيبها فيما هي ليست بكاذبة فأطاعها، فحق مثله الإغضاء عنه، لأنه دون كل مقلد. فإن قال بالوجهين الآخرين فقد أكذب نفسه فيها حكى عنها . والله الموفق.
Then he said: 'We do not say of the fasiq upon investigation: he is not a believer, rather we do not name him with it.' So it is said to him: Is he in reality a believer, or not a believer, or neither believer nor disbeliever? If he says the first, then he is a man whose soul called him to deny it in what it is not lying, so he obeyed it; so the fitting treatment for the likes of him is to overlook him, because he is below every blind follower. If he says the other two ways, then he has belied himself in what he reported from it. And Allah is the Granter of success.
Source pages p. 438

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical argument about the fasiq (grave sinner). The opponent says 'we do not call him a believer nor do we say he is not a believer.' The author then forces a trichotomy: either he is a believer, or not a believer, or neither. If the opponent chooses the first, he is contradicting himself by denying his own statement that the fasiq is not named believer. If he chooses either of the other two, he contradicts himself in what he narrated from himself. Thus the opponent is trapped between contradictions. The author recommends overlooking the first type because he is worse than a blind follower (تقليد). 'Allah is the Granter of success' ends the argument.

مؤمن
تحقيق
مقلد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that not naming the fasiq a believer is a safe, neutral position that avoids theological error.
Correction: Maturidi shows that this position is logically inconsistent because it leads to self-contradiction when forced into a clear classification.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi conclude about a person who says the fasiq is in reality a believer?
A) He is correct
B) He is a blind follower
C) He should be overlooked because he is below every blind follower
D) He deserves punishment

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that refusing to classify the fasiq as either believer or disbeliever results in logical self-contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a position on a matter of faith without examining its logical consequences, simply because it felt comfortable? What would it cost you to submit to the truth even if it means admitting you were wrong? Will you commit today to investigating your beliefs with honesty, no matter where it leads?
?
The opponent in this passage followed his soul's desire to deny what he knew to be true. In what area of your life are you avoiding calling something by its real name because it is uncomfortable—whether in your own character, your relationships, or your practice of Islam? When will you stop and name it as it is?
?
The blind follower at least follows someone else's knowledge, but the opponent followed his own denial. Are you willing to be taught by those who know more than you, or do you prefer to hold onto your own reasoning even when it leads to contradiction? What step can you take today to humble yourself before a scholar or a teacher?

Refuting the Claim of Consensus on Promise Verses
Maturidi refutes the alleged consensus on promise verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the claim that there is consensus on the generality of promise verses and why he considers it a lie.

Core Idea
Maturidi exposes the claim of consensus as a false refuge with Quranic evidence.

Before we explore Maturidi's three positive answers, we must first see how he dismantles the opponent's reliance on a false consensus. The opponent had argued that all promise verses are general for every believer. Maturidi responds sharply, calling this claim a lie and proving it from the Qur'an itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the opponent's argument and Maturidi's initial refutation.
Notice the strong language: 'kadhib' (lie).
2
Identify the opponent's evidence
What verse does the opponent cite? How does he justify the claim of consensus?
The opponent uses Quran 33:47 and claims consensus on 'am al-wa'd'.
3
Analyze Maturidi's counter-proof
Why does Maturidi cite Quran 5:85? How does it refute the opponent's point?
The verse shows reward is specific to 'what they said', not any speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم احتج بقوله : وبشر المؤمنين بأن لهم من الله فضلاً كبيرًا ﴾ [الأحزاب، ٤٧/٣٣]، ونحو ذلك من الوعد للمؤمنين باسم المطلق، ثم لم يكن ذلك لمرتكب الفسق فيه، وقد أجمع على عموم الوعد وإن اختلف في عموم الوعيد. قال الشيخ رحمه الله : } نقول وبالله التوفيق : دعواه الإجماع في عموم آيات الوعد كذب لا يزال يجعله مفزعا لنفسه في كل نائبة، وقد قال الله : فأثابهم الله بما قالوا جنات الآية" ، وليس يجب مثله للقول خاصة في قول الجميع، فثبت به كذبه في الحكاية. ثم لما استدل بالآيات أجوبة ثلاثة. أحدها الإخبار عن منتهى الأحوال، وإلى ذلك مرجع كل مؤمن . والثاني أن يكون الوعد للذين حققوا الإيمان بأخلاقه ، وما دل عليه ؛ وجائز تسمية أحد بشيء، ذلك اسم لأمر تتصل به أمور عند جمع تلك الأمور ، ويجوز أيضا عند إفراد الاسم بالذي به سمّى . والله أعلم. والثالث أن يكون له ذلك الجزاء وما يُعاقب به فهو يعاقب بحقوقه " ، وليس الذي يصيبه بحق دينه مقصرًا عنه حق دينه . مع ما بين الله أن له الله فضلاً كبيراً ، ليُعلم أن الجزاء للخيرات منه إفضال ؛ وما كان حكمة الإفضال فإلى من يقوم به ذلك يكون من اختيار أحوال وأوقات .
Then he argued by His saying: {And give glad tidings to the believers that they will have from Allah a great bounty} [Quran 33:47] and similar promises to believers by the absolute name, then that was not for the perpetrator of sin in it, and there is consensus on the generality of the promise although they differed on the generality of the threat. The Shaykh (may Allah have mercy on him) said: 'We say, and with Allah is success: His claim of consensus on the generality of the verses of promise is a lie; he never ceases to make it a refuge for himself in every calamity. And Allah said: {So Allah rewarded them for what they said with gardens} [Quran 5:85], and the like of that is not necessary for speech especially in the statement of all; thus his falsehood in the narration is established. Then when he argued with the verses, there are three answers. First: It is informing about the ultimate states, and to that is the reference of every believer. Second: That the promise is for those who realized faith through its morals and what it indicates; and it is permissible to name someone by something, that name is for a matter to which matters are connected when those matters are gathered, and it is also permissible when isolating the name by that by which he is named. And Allah knows best. Third: That he has that recompense, and what he is punished with, he is punished by his entitlements. And what befalls him by right of his religion is not deficient from the right of his religion. Along with what Allah made clear that for him from Allah is a great bounty, so that it be known that the reward for good deeds is a favor from Him; and what is the wisdom of favor? It goes to whoever performs it, being from the choice of states and times.'
Source pages p. 438

Commentary
This passage presents an opponent's argument that there is consensus that verses of promise (like 33:47) are general for all believers, including sinners, though threat verses may differ. Maturidi refutes this as a lie, pointing to Quran 5:85 which rewards people for saying something, showing that promise is not simply for any speech. Then he gives three answers: (1) These verses inform about the final state in Paradise, which every believer reaches eventually. (2) The promise is for those who actualize faith through its moral traits; the name 'believer' is given to one who fulfills all associated matters, but isolated use of the name may still be permissible. (3) The sinner's punishment is just, not a deprivation of religion's right; rather any good recompense is a favor (إفضال) from Allah, not automatic. The wisdom of favor depends on divine choice.

Key Terms
وعد
وعيد
إجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that promise verses apply unconditionally to every believer, based on an alleged consensus.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the claimed consensus is false and that reward is conditional on specific deeds, using Quran 5:85.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi use to refute the claim of consensus?
A) Logical reasoning
B) Quran verse 5:85
C) Consensus of scholars
D) The threat verses

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes the alleged consensus on promise verses by citing Quran 5:85, which ties reward to specific deeds.

Maturidi calls the opponent's claim a 'lie'. Is there any area of your faith where you have uncritically accepted a consensus? Will you seek out the primary evidence yourself?
?
God's promise is conditional on your actions. What specific deeds are you relying on to earn His reward, and are they truly aligned with His commands? Are you counting on general promises while neglecting the conditions?

First and Second Answers: Ultimate States and Qualified Faith
Maturidi gives the first and second answers to interpret promise verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's first and second explanations of how promise verses apply to believers: as news about final outcomes and as conditional on actualized faith.

Core Idea
Promise verses describe the ultimate reward and apply only to those who truly embody faith.

Having refuted the false consensus, Maturidi now offers constructive interpretations of the promise verses. His first answer clarifies that these verses inform about the final state of believers in Paradise. His second answer adds that the promise is for those who actualize faith through its moral implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage containing the first two answers
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the two distinct answers.
Note the enumeration: 'أحدها' and 'والثاني'.
2
Distinguish between the two answers
What is the first answer about? What is the second?
First: informing about ultimate states. Second: promise for those who realized faith.
3
Understand the naming nuance
Reflect on the point about naming a person by a name that implies a set of attributes.
This clarifies that 'believer' is a comprehensive term.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم احتج بقوله : وبشر المؤمنين بأن لهم من الله فضلاً كبيرًا ﴾ [الأحزاب، ٤٧/٣٣]، ونحو ذلك من الوعد للمؤمنين باسم المطلق، ثم لم يكن ذلك لمرتكب الفسق فيه، وقد أجمع على عموم الوعد وإن اختلف في عموم الوعيد. قال الشيخ رحمه الله : } نقول وبالله التوفيق : دعواه الإجماع في عموم آيات الوعد كذب لا يزال يجعله مفزعا لنفسه في كل نائبة، وقد قال الله : فأثابهم الله بما قالوا جنات الآية" ، وليس يجب مثله للقول خاصة في قول الجميع، فثبت به كذبه في الحكاية. ثم لما استدل بالآيات أجوبة ثلاثة. أحدها الإخبار عن منتهى الأحوال، وإلى ذلك مرجع كل مؤمن . والثاني أن يكون الوعد للذين حققوا الإيمان بأخلاقه ، وما دل عليه ؛ وجائز تسمية أحد بشيء، ذلك اسم لأمر تتصل به أمور عند جمع تلك الأمور ، ويجوز أيضا عند إفراد الاسم بالذي به سمّى . والله أعلم. والثالث أن يكون له ذلك الجزاء وما يُعاقب به فهو يعاقب بحقوقه " ، وليس الذي يصيبه بحق دينه مقصرًا عنه حق دينه . مع ما بين الله أن له الله فضلاً كبيراً ، ليُعلم أن الجزاء للخيرات منه إفضال ؛ وما كان حكمة الإفضال فإلى من يقوم به ذلك يكون من اختيار أحوال وأوقات .
Then he argued by His saying: {And give glad tidings to the believers that they will have from Allah a great bounty} [Quran 33:47] and similar promises to believers by the absolute name, then that was not for the perpetrator of sin in it, and there is consensus on the generality of the promise although they differed on the generality of the threat. The Shaykh (may Allah have mercy on him) said: 'We say, and with Allah is success: His claim of consensus on the generality of the verses of promise is a lie; he never ceases to make it a refuge for himself in every calamity. And Allah said: {So Allah rewarded them for what they said with gardens} [Quran 5:85], and the like of that is not necessary for speech especially in the statement of all; thus his falsehood in the narration is established. Then when he argued with the verses, there are three answers. First: It is informing about the ultimate states, and to that is the reference of every believer. Second: That the promise is for those who realized faith through its morals and what it indicates; and it is permissible to name someone by something, that name is for a matter to which matters are connected when those matters are gathered, and it is also permissible when isolating the name by that by which he is named. And Allah knows best. Third: That he has that recompense, and what he is punished with, he is punished by his entitlements. And what befalls him by right of his religion is not deficient from the right of his religion. Along with what Allah made clear that for him from Allah is a great bounty, so that it be known that the reward for good deeds is a favor from Him; and what is the wisdom of favor? It goes to whoever performs it, being from the choice of states and times.'
Source pages p. 438

Commentary
This passage presents an opponent's argument that there is consensus that verses of promise (like 33:47) are general for all believers, including sinners, though threat verses may differ. Maturidi refutes this as a lie, pointing to Quran 5:85 which rewards people for saying something, showing that promise is not simply for any speech. Then he gives three answers: (1) These verses inform about the final state in Paradise, which every believer reaches eventually. (2) The promise is for those who actualize faith through its moral traits; the name 'believer' is given to one who fulfills all associated matters, but isolated use of the name may still be permissible. (3) The sinner's punishment is just, not a deprivation of religion's right; rather any good recompense is a favor (إفضال) from Allah, not automatic. The wisdom of favor depends on divine choice.

Key Terms
وعد
إيمان
أخلاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that simply being called a believer in the Qur'an guarantees the promise without conditions.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the promise is conditional on actualizing faith through its morals.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's second answer, to whom does the promise apply?
A) All who call themselves believers
B) Those who realized faith through its morals
C) Only prophets
D) Sinners who repent

Key Takeaway
The promise verses refer to the final outcome and are for those who actualize faith, not for nominal believers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you only claim to be a believer by name, or have you actualized faith through its moral characteristics as described by Maturidi? Identify one area where your actions do not reflect your faith and commit to change this week.
?
When you hear verses promising Paradise to believers, do you feel entitled to it based on your label, or do you see it as a call to embody the morals of faith? What moral trait do you most lack?
?
If the promise of Paradise is for those who realize faith through its morals, how would you assess your own life? What step can you take today to bring your character closer to the description of a true believer?

Third Answer: Divine Reward as Pure Favor
Maturidi presents the third answer: reward is divine favor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's third answer: that the recompense for good deeds is a favor (إفضال) from Allah, not an automatic entitlement, and that punishment is justly deserved.

Core Idea
Reward is a divine favor, while punishment is just; no one is deprived of their religious due.

Maturidi's third answer illuminates the relationship between human action and divine recompense. He argues that the punishment incurred by a sinner is just, while any reward is a pure favor from Allah. This clarifies that verses of promise do not guarantee automatic benefit for every believer.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage containing the third answer

Note the key term 'إفضال' (favor).
2
Understand the justice of punishment
Why does Maturidi say the sinner is punished 'bil-huquq' (by his rights)?
Punishment is just and does not deprive him of his religious right.
3
Reflect on the nature of reward as favor
Why is reward described as a favor? What does this imply about promise verses?
Reward is not automatic but a gracious gift from Allah.

The Threat for the Fasiq and False Attribution to the Murji'ah
Maturidi dismisses a false attribution and states the threat for the unrepentant fasiq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Maturidi refutes a false claim against the Murji'ah and clarifies their position on the threat for the unrepentant fasiq.

Core Idea
Even the Murji'ah affirm divine threat for sinners who die impenitent.

Maturidi addresses a common distortion: some critics fabricate sayings to attack the Murji'ah. He dismisses such lies as unworthy of mention. Then he clarifies an important point: despite their emphasis on faith alone, the Murji'ah do not deny the threat (وعيد) for a fasiq who dies without repentance. This passage helps correct misunderstandings about this theological group.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic once, then the translation. Notice the structure: first a dismissal, then an assertion.
The bracketed [خلاف] indicates textual uncertainty, but the meaning is clear.
2
Understand the dismissal
Maturidi says he left out a fabricated saying because it is a lie and of no benefit. This shows his scholarly integrity.
3
Grasp the agreement on threat
He then states that it is established by agreement (with one opponent) that the threat applies to the unrepentant fasiq among the people of irja'.
This underscores that even the Murji'ah do not suspend punishment entirely.
4

This connects to the learning objective.

Then he said: Since it is established by agreed statement – except for one [opposing] – that the threat for the fasiq if he dies before repentance is among the people of irja'.
Source pages p. 439

Commentary
This passage begins by dismissing a fabricated saying falsely attributed to the Murji'ah (a theological group). The author considers this a lie and unworthy of mention. Then he asserts that it is established by consensus, with one dissenter, that the threat (وعيد) applies to the fasiq (grave sinner) who dies without repentance – a position held by the people of irja' (the Murji'ah). This clarifies that even the Murji'ah, known for suspending judgment on sinners, still affirm the threat for the unrepentant fasiq.

Refutation of Abandoning Minor Sins as Faith
Maturidi refutes the claim that avoiding minor sins is faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that avoiding minor sins cannot be equated with faith, because it leads to a logical inconsistency regarding punishment.

Core Idea
If avoiding minor sins were faith, it would remain even during major sins—contradicting punishment.

In this brief passage, Imam Maturidi refutes a common but flawed claim: that simply refraining from minor sins constitutes faith. He demonstrates that this reasoning collapses under scrutiny, because it cannot account for punishment when major sins are committed. Let's examine his logical rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Let the words settle before analyzing.
Notice the concise style of Maturidi's refutation.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent argues that avoiding minor sins is faith because one is punished for minor sins if major sins are not avoided. Find this claim in the text.
3
Follow Maturidi's response
Maturidi presents a dilemma: if avoiding minor sins is faith, then it should be faith even when committing major sins, so punishment should not occur. But the opponent says punishment happens. Hence contradiction.
Consider the second scenario: when major sins are avoided, the opponent does not consider avoiding minor sins as faith, which is also inconsistent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم زعم أن ترك الصغائر إيمان لما يعاقب عليها لو لم يجتنب الكبائر. فيقال له: إذا يجب أن يكون إيمانا عند ارتكاب الكبائر، لم يعذب على ضد ذلك، وليس بإيمان إذا اجتنب؟ وذلك غاية الحيرة.
Then he claimed that abandoning minor sins is faith because one is punished for them if one does not avoid major sins. So it is said to him: If it must be faith when committing major sins, then he would not be punished for the opposite of that, and is it not faith when he avoids [them]? That is the utmost confusion.
Source pages p. 440

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that avoiding minor sins is itself faith, based on the idea that one is punished for minor sins if major sins are not avoided. Maturidi points out the inconsistency: if avoiding minor sins were faith, then even when committing major sins, the person would still have that faith (since he is avoiding minor sins), so he should not be punished. But the opponent's position is that such a person is punished, meaning that avoiding minor sins cannot be faith. Furthermore, if he avoids major sins, then by the same logic, avoiding minor sins should also be faith, but the opponent denies that. This is contradictory and shows confusion.

Key Terms
صغائر
كبائر
إيمان
عقاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That avoiding minor sins is itself faith.
Correction: Maturidi shows that such a view leads to inconsistency regarding punishment; faith is a more comprehensive concept.

Checkpoint
Maturidi refutes the claim that abandoning minor sins is faith by pointing out that:
A) Minor sins are not punished
B) If it were faith, it would remain even when committing major sins
C) Faith is only in the heart
D) Major sins cancel all faith

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that equating any action with faith leads to contradictions when considering punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a major sin, do you still consider yourself a person of faith? According to this lesson, if faith were merely avoiding minor sins, you would have no faith in that moment. What does that reveal about where you might be placing your trust—in your own actions or in Allah's mercy? Will you examine your heart to see if you rely more on your deeds or on Allah's grace?
?
Have you ever thought that simply avoiding certain 'small' sins is enough to secure your faith, while neglecting greater acts of obedience? This lesson shows that such thinking leads to confusion. Are you willing to rethink what faith truly means and let go of any false sense of security? What change will you make today?
?
The opponent's logic led to a contradiction: a person could have faith and still be punished. Have you ever felt conflicted about your own faith when you sin? This lesson challenges you to not equate faith with actions. Will you instead strengthen your inner conviction and seek forgiveness for your shortcomings, knowing that faith is deeper than mere avoidance? What step will you take to realign your understanding?

Clarifying the Meaning of 'Fajir' in the Forgiveness Argument
Maturidi questions the ambiguity of 'fajir' in the forgiveness argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi exposes the ambiguity of the term 'fajir' to refute an argument about seeking forgiveness.

Core Idea
The meaning of a key term can make or break an argument.

In theological debates, the meaning of terms is crucial. Maturidi here challenges an opponent who claims that the Muslim community forbids seeking forgiveness for the 'fajir'. By asking for clarification, Maturidi shows that the argument is either irrelevant or based on a false premise.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Notice the concise style and the logical structure.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the two possible meanings of 'fajir'
Maturidi asks the opponent: do you mean a disbeliever or a believer who commits a major sin without deeming it lawful? Understand both possibilities.
3
Understand the refutation
See how each meaning leads to a problem for the opponent. If the first, it's irrelevant; if the second, it's false. Thus the argument fails.
This shows the importance of clear definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم احتج بما لا يجوز عند الأمة الاستغفار للفاجر. فيقال له: ما تعنى بالفاجر: الكافر، أو الذي يرتكب كبيرة في حال إيمان بلا استحلال؟ فإن قال بالأول حاد وإن قال بالثاني كذب على الأمة، وجعله دليلا لاستحقاقه الخلود في النار بما ظهر من صنيعه.
Then he argued with what the community does not allow: seeking forgiveness for the wicked [fajir]. So it is said to him: What do you mean by 'fajir': the disbeliever, or one who commits a major sin while in a state of faith without deeming it lawful? If he says the first, he deviates; if he says the second, he lies against the community, and makes it evidence for his deserving eternity in the Fire by what appears from his action.
Source pages p. 440

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an argument that uses the claim that the Muslim community (al-ummah) does not permit seeking forgiveness for the wicked (fajir). Maturidi challenges the opponent to clarify what he means by 'fajir'. If he means a disbeliever (kafir), then the ruling is different and not applicable to believers. If he means a believer who commits a major sin without deeming it lawful, then the claim that the community forbids seeking forgiveness for such a person is false, as Muslims do seek forgiveness for sinful believers. Thus, the opponent's argument is either irrelevant or based on a false premise. Maturidi concludes that the opponent has made this a proof for the sinner's eternal damnation, but it is invalid.

Key Terms
فاجر
استغفار
أمة
خلود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might assume that 'fajir' always means a sinful believer, making the opponent's argument seem plausible.
Correction: Maturidi shows that 'fajir' can also mean disbeliever, and even if it means sinful believer, the community does seek forgiveness for them.

Checkpoint
In the argument about seeking forgiveness for the fajir, Maturidi asks the opponent to clarify whether 'fajir' means:
A) A disbeliever or a believer who commits a major sin without deeming it lawful
B) Any Muslim
C) Only hypocrites
D) A person who leaves prayer

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes an argument by exposing the ambiguity of the term 'fajir', showing that it is either irrelevant or false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you label someone as 'wicked' or sinful, do you ever pause to define what you mean? How might this lesson expose your own tendency to judge others vaguely and dismiss their hope in God's mercy?
?
Think of a disagreement you've had where both sides used the same word but meant different things. How could you apply Maturidi's method of clarifying terms to that situation? Will you seek to clarify before concluding?
?
Have you ever used a theological argument to justify a harsh belief about others (e.g., that certain sinners are beyond forgiveness) without checking the precise meaning of your terms? This lesson shows that ambiguity can make your argument false. Will you now examine your beliefs with more precision?
