Refuting the Claim that Abandoning Minor Sins is Faith
Maturidi refutes the claim that leaving minor sins constitutes faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical inconsistency in the claim that avoiding minor sins constitutes faith.

Core Idea
Avoiding minor sins does not equal faith; that reasoning leads to confusion.

Some argued that because one must avoid minor sins, doing so is itself faith. But this reasoning collapses when examined closely. Maturidi exposes the flaw by showing it leads to absurd conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's claim
The opponent says that leaving minor sins is faith because otherwise they would be punishable if major sins are not avoided. Understand the logic behind this claim.
The opponent tries to equate an action (avoidance) with a state (faith).
2
Identify the refutation
Maturidi responds by pointing out that if the reasoning were sound, then committing major sins would also have to be faith? And one would not be punished for its opposite. This is the key inconsistency.
Maturidi's method is to show the argument leads to confusion.
3
Analyze the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that this is 'utmost confusion'. Why does he use such strong language? Because the opponent's claim undermines clear theological distinctions.
Faith is not merely avoiding sins, but includes belief and other conditions.
4
Apply the lesson
Consider the broader implication: faith cannot be reduced to any single action. This refutation protects the comprehensive nature of faith in Islam.
This is a common issue in theological debates—oversimplifying faith.

ثم زعم أن ترك الصغائر إيمان لما يعاقب عليها لو لم يجتنب الكبائر. فيقال له: إذا يجب أن يكون إيمانًا عند ارتكاب الكبائر، لم يعذب على ضد ذلك، وليس بإيمان إذا اجتنب؟ وذلك غاية الحيرة.
Then he claimed that abandoning minor sins is faith because one would be punished for them if one did not avoid major sins. So it is said to him: If it must be that it is faith when committing major sins, why is one not punished for the opposite of that, and it is not faith when one avoids? That is the utmost confusion.
Source pages p. 440

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The opponent claims that leaving minor sins is considered faith because otherwise they would be punishable if major sins are not avoided. Maturidi refutes by pointing out the inconsistency: if that reasoning holds, then committing major sins would also be considered faith? And why would one be punished for the opposite? He concludes that this is the utmost confusion (غاية الحيرة), indicating the logical flaw in the opponent's position.

Refuting the Claim Against Seeking Forgiveness for a Sinner
Maturidi refutes the claim that seeking forgiveness for a fajir is impermissible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of the claim that seeking forgiveness for a fajir is impermissible, including his clarification of the term and his two arguments against the opponent.

Core Idea
The term 'fajir' is ambiguous; clarifying it refutes the claim against seeking forgiveness.

Maturidi addresses an objection that the community does not seek forgiveness for an immoral person. He first forces the opponent to define their terms, revealing a dilemma. Then he presents two additional problems with the opponent's position. This lesson walks through his structured refutation.

Guided Path
1
Clarifying the term 'fajir'
The passage begins by asking what the opponent means by fajir. Notice the two options and their consequences: if it means disbeliever, the opponent deviates; if it means a Muslim sinner, he lies about the community.
This step sets up the entire refutation.
2
Examining the two objections
Maturidi then raises two points against the opponent's claim. The first point concerns inconsistent labeling of immorality and faith. The second point criticizes limiting prophetic forgiveness to the already forgiven.
3
Understanding the implications
Reflect on how this refutation protects the practice of seeking forgiveness for Muslim sinners and upholds prophetic practice. Consider the broader principle of using clear definitions in theological debate.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم احتج بما لا يجوز عند الأمة الاستغفار للفاجر. فيقال له: ما تعنى بالفاجر: الكافر، أو الذي يرتكب كبيرة في حال إيمان بلا استحلال؟ فإن قال بالأول حاد، وإن قال بالثاني كذب على الأمة، وجعله دليلاً لاستحقاقه الخلود في النار بما ظهر من صنيعه، فمبذول له ما تمنى في نفسه. ثم عليه فيه أمران: أحدهما إطلاق الاسم بالفجور مرة، وهو حالة فيه ولا فعل فجور، تجوزاً بإطلاق الاسم بالإيمان، وهو فيه ومعه حقيقة فعله بالسمع والعقل جميعا. بل لا يجوز إطلاق اسم الفجور حتى يبين، وجائز ذلك في الإيمان بما جاء به القرآن واتفق عليه أهل اللسان. ولا قوة إلا بالله. والثاني أنه صرف استغفار الأنبياء والأولياء إلى ما هو مغفور، وذلك كتمان نعمة الغفران وإعراض عن الشكر فيها ذلك حقه، وذلك بعيد ممتنع. والله الموفق.
Then he argued by [the claim] that seeking forgiveness for the immoral person (fajir) is not permissible according to the community. So it is said to him: What do you mean by the immoral person: the disbeliever, or one who commits a major sin while in a state of faith without considering it lawful? If he says the first, he deviates; if he says the second, he lies against the community, and he makes it a proof of his deserving eternal fire because of his apparent action, so what he desires in himself is given to him. Then there are two points against him concerning it: One is that he applies the name of immorality once, while it is a state in him and no act of immorality, as a license by applying the name of faith, while it is in him and with him in reality of his act by both revelation and reason. Rather, it is not permissible to apply the name of immorality until it is clarified, and that is permissible concerning faith according to what the Qur'an came with and the people of language agreed upon. And there is no power except with God. The second is that he diverts the seeking of forgiveness of the prophets and saints to what is already forgiven, and that is concealing the grace of forgiveness and turning away from gratitude therein which is its due, and that is far and impossible. And God is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 440

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer followed by a detailed refutation in two points. The opponent argues that it is not permissible for the umma to seek forgiveness for a fajir. Maturidi first clarifies the term: if by fajir he means a disbeliever, then he deviates from proper usage; if he means a Muslim who commits a major sin without considering it lawful, then he lies about the umma, as the umma does seek forgiveness for such people. Then Maturidi raises two problems: (1) The opponent inconsistently applies the label of immorality based on a single act while the person may be in a state of faith. The application of such labels should be based on clear evidence, and the same standard should be applied to faith. (2) The opponent limits the seeking of forgiveness by prophets and saints to only those already forgiven, which negates the purpose of seeking forgiveness as an act of gratitude and recognition of God's grace. This is impossible.

Key Terms
الفاجر
الاستغفار
إطلاق الاسم
الكافر
الكبيرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that seeking forgiveness for a known sinner is inappropriate or impermissible.
Correction: Maturidi shows that seeking forgiveness for a fajir is permissible, and clarifies that prophets and saints do seek forgiveness for sinners.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is problematic about the opponent's claim that seeking forgiveness for a fajir is not allowed?
A) The opponent defines fajir clearly.
B) The opponent's definition is ambiguous, leading to either deviation or lying.
C) The opponent ignores the Sunnah.
D) The opponent only refers to hypocrites.

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of seeking forgiveness for a fajir is defended by clarifying the term and showing that prophets and saints seek forgiveness for sinners.

Refuting the Qadf Verse Argument
Maturidi refutes the qadf verse argument by highlighting conditional curse and context.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the claim that a cursed person cannot be a believer by analyzing the conditional nature of the curse in the verse of qadf.

Core Idea
The curse in the Qur'an is conditional, not absolute—context matters.

Maturidi responds to an opponent who uses a verse about false accusation to claim that anyone cursed cannot be a believer. Maturidi carefully examines the actual wording of the verse and points out the opponent's mistakes. This lesson unpacks his refutation, showing the importance of precise Qur'anic interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully to understand the flow of Maturidi's argument.
Notice the opponent's claim and Maturidi's immediate response.
2
Identify the three refutation points
Maturidi makes three key points: the curse is conditional, the opponent's inconsistency, and the verse's context about hypocrites. Identify each.
3
Reflect on the method
Consider why Maturidi starts with accusing the opponent of lying against the Qur’ān and how he uses internal consistency.
This shows a method of refutation by pointing out misquotation and inconsistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم احتج بآية القذف أن الله أخبر أنهم ملعونون بلا ذكر استحلال وغيره، ولا يكون الملعون مؤمناً. نقول، وبالله التوفيق: إنما في الآية لعنة الله عليه إن كان كذلك، وليس فيها أن الله سماه، فأول ما في اعتلالك أن كذبت على القرآن. ثم كيف ألزمته اللعنة بقوله: إن كان من الكاذبين، ولم تلزمه اسم الإيمان؟ وهو يقول به ويحقق. وأيضا إنه رد أحد اللعنتين إلى الحد، فكذلك الآخر. على أن الآية نزلت في المنافقين.
Then he argued by the verse of false accusation that God informed that they are cursed without mention of considering it lawful or otherwise, and the cursed cannot be a believer. We say, and with God is success: Indeed in the verse is 'the curse of God upon him if he is among the liars', and it does not contain that God named him [as cursed unconditionally]. The first thing in your reasoning is that you lied against the Qur'an. Then how do you make the curse binding upon him by His saying 'if he is among the liars', while you do not make the name of faith binding upon him? And he affirms it and actualizes it. And also, he referred one of the two curses to the prescribed punishment, so likewise the other. Moreover, the verse was revealed concerning the hypocrites.
Source pages p. 441

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer concerning the verse of qadf (false accusation) from Surah al-Nur. The opponent argues that the verse says those who accuse chaste women are cursed without any condition, and a cursed person cannot be a believer. Maturidi refutes by pointing out that the actual verse (24:7) says 'the curse of God be upon him if he is of the liars'—the curse is conditional. The opponent is lying against the Qur'an. Furthermore, if the curse is conditional on being a liar, then why does the opponent not make the name of faith conditional? The opponent also inconsistently treats one of the two curses (in the li'an verse) as leading to the prescribed punishment (hadd), so the other should be treated similarly. Finally, the verse was revealed about hypocrites, not about believers.

Key Terms
آية القذف
اللعنة
الملعون
المؤمن
المنافقون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A cursed person cannot be a believer under any circumstances.
Correction: The curse in the qadf verse is conditional on being a liar, and the verse was revealed about hypocrites, not believers.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi refute the claim that those cursed in the qadf verse are not believers?
A) He says the curse is unconditional.
B) He says the verse applies only to disbelievers.
C) He points out that the curse is conditional on being a liar.
D) He says the verse is abrogated.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that the qadf verse does not prove that a cursed person cannot be a believer; the curse is conditional and the verse applies to hypocrites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you read the conditional curse 'if he is among the liars', does your heart tremble at the possibility that your own tongue could bring such a curse upon you? What statements have you made that were not true, even in jest, and how can you repent sincerely today?
?
The opponent twisted the Qur'an to support his view. Are there any verses you have taken out of context or interpreted to justify your own desires, rather than submissing to Allah's intention? How can you ensure you are not 'lying against the Qur'an'?
?
This passage shows that even a harsh verse like that was revealed about hypocrites. Do you ever consider that your own actions might resemble hypocrisy? Have you examined your faith's consistency, and what step will you take this week to purify your intentions?

Neglect of Prayer and Hijrah: Sins, Not Kufr
Maturidi defines neglect of prayer as rejection and uses hijrah to show obligations do not remove iman.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that neglecting obligatory acts like prayer or hijrah does not remove one from faith, as long as the obligation is not rejected.

Core Idea
Neglect of prayer is rejection of its obligation, not delay; missing hijrah does not erase iman.

Imam Maturidi addresses a critical misunderstanding: do major sins like neglecting prayer or not emigrating nullify faith? He shows that verses warning about these acts refer to rejection of the obligation, not mere failure to perform. This distinction preserves the name of iman even when obligations are left undone.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice how Maturidi defines 'ida'a' (neglect) and uses hijrah as an example.
2
Identify the key definitions
Find where Maturidi distinguishes between rejection (radd) and delay (ta'akhir). This is central.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this principle applies to other obligations. Does failing to pray remove someone from Islam? Maturidi says no, unless they reject the obligation.
This connects to the broader Maturidi position on major sins.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومثله في احتجاجه بقوله : أضاعوا الصلاة " الآية " ؛ مع ما قال الله تعالى: ﴿فإن تابوا وأقاموا الصلاة" الآية، وما ذكر من الأخوة والتخلية" واجب بالقبول دون الفعل، فكذلك الإضاعة تكون بالرد دون التأخير. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وقد قال الله تعالى: ﴿ والذين آمنوا ولم يهاجروا ما لكم من ولايتهم من شيء حتى يهاجروا] [الأنفال، ۷۲/۸] وقال: ﴿ولا تقولوا لمن ألقى إليكم السلام لست مؤمنا ﴾ [النساء، ٩٤/٤]؛ ثبت أن الذي يقوله " لا يزيل الإيمان ولا اسمه. والله أعلم. وقد احتج بقوله : فإن تابوا وأقاموا الصلاة ﴾ [التوبة، ٥/٩ ، ١١] الآية ، لكننا بينا أن ذلك في حق القبول؛ إذ لو ينتظر الفعل به لكان لا يجب الأخوة أبدا ولا يخلى سبيلهم، وفي [ذلك] الإعياء إلى حول، وذلك مما لا معنى له . و [أيضا] قد بينا أمر الهجرة، وأنها كانت من الفرائض التي جاء في التخلف عنها الوعيد الشديد، ثم قد أثبت اسم الإيمان مع عدم ذلك. والله أعلم.
And similarly his arguing by His saying: "They neglected the prayer" – along with what Allah said: "If they repent and establish prayer..." and what was mentioned of brotherhood and release is obligatory by acceptance, not by action. So likewise, negligence (ida'a) is by rejection (radd), not by delay. And there is no power except with Allah. And Allah said: "And those who believed but did not emigrate – you have no guardianship over them until they emigrate" [8:72] and He said: "And do not say to the one who gives you the greeting, 'You are not a believer'" [4:94]. It is established that what he says does not remove faith nor its name. And Allah knows best. And he argued by His saying: "If they repent and establish prayer..." [9:5,11], but we have explained that that concerns acceptance; for if action were awaited, brotherhood would never be obligatory, nor would they be released, and that would involve delay for a year, which is meaningless. And also we have explained the matter of hijrah, that it was among the obligations for which severe warning came for neglecting it, yet the name of iman is affirmed despite its absence. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 442

Commentary
This passage refutes the view that the verse "They neglected the prayer" (Surah Maryam 19:59) indicates kufr. Maturidi argues that neglect (ida'a) means rejection (radd) of the obligation, not merely delaying it. Similarly, the verse about brotherhood after repentance and establishing prayer (Surah At-Tawbah) refers to acceptance of the offer of peace, not to actual performance of prayer as a condition for brotherhood. He also uses the verse about emigration (hijrah) to show that even though hijrah was a duty and its abandonment incurs severe warning, the believers who did not emigrate are still called believers (see Surah An-Nisa 4:94). Therefore, abandoning an obligatory act does not remove the name of iman.

Key Terms
إضاعة الصلاة
الرد
الهجرة
الأخوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Neglecting the prayer or not performing hijrah removes one from the fold of Islam.
Correction: Maturidi shows that such neglect is only kufr if it involves rejection of the obligation; mere failure to act does not negate iman.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi mean by "إضاعة الصلاة" (neglect of prayer)?
A) Delay in prayer
B) Rejection of prayer's obligation
C) Forgetting prayer
D) Not praying in congregation

Key Takeaway
Neglect of prayer and hijrah are major sins but do not remove faith unless the obligation itself is rejected.

Based on this lesson, what is the real line between a sin that harms faith and a sin that destroys it? Will you change how you view your own slips?

Faith Remains Even Without Actions
Maturidi argues that faith remains even when actions are not performed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that faith is not invalidated by the neglect of obligatory actions, based on Quranic evidence and rational reasoning.

Core Idea
Faith is upheld by acceptance, not by performing every act.

This lesson examines a key Maturidi argument: that faith remains intact even when obligatory actions are not performed. Imam Maturidi refutes the claim that neglecting duties removes faith, using Quranic verses and logical analysis. You will explore the distinction between acceptance and action, and see how the Qur'an itself affirms faith for those who did not emigrate or who greet others as believers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the main claim: faith is not removed by leaving actions.
Pay attention to the distinction between 'acceptance' (qabul) and 'act' (fi'l).
2
Understand the key distinction
Maturidi distinguishes between obligation by acceptance and obligation by action. Wasting (ida'a) is rejection, not delay. Grasp this foundation.
3
Examine the Quranic evidence
Look at the verses on emigration (al-Anfal 72) and greeting (al-Nisa 94). How do they show that faith remains even when emigration is not performed?
The emigration verse shows faith is affirmed despite lack of emigration.
4
Analyze the refutation
Maturidi refutes the opponent's proof from al-Tawbah 5,11. Understand why he says it is about acceptance, not the act itself.
If the act were required, brotherhood would never become obligatory—an absurdity.
5
Synthesize the conclusion
Combine all evidence: faith's name remains even when duties are unfulfilled. How does this shape your understanding of faith?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصلاة " الآية"، وما ذكر من الأخوة والتخلية" واجب بالقبول دون الفعل، فكذلك الإضاعة تكون بالرد دون التأخير. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وقد قال الله تعالى: ﴿ والذين آمنوا ولم يهاجروا ما لكم من ولايتهم من شيء حتى يهاجروا] [الأنفال، ۷۲/۸] وقال: ﴿ولا تقولوا لمن ألقى إليكم السلام لست مؤمنا ﴾ [النساء، ٩٤/٤]؛ ثبت أن الذي يقوله " لا يزيل الإيمان ولا اسمه. والله أعلم. وقد احتج بقوله : فإن تابوا وأقاموا الصلاة ﴾ [التوبة، ٥/٩ ، ١١] الآية ، لكننا بينا أن ذلك في حق القبول؛ إذ لو ينتظر الفعل به لكان لا يجب الأخوة أبدا ولا يخلى سبيلهم، وفي [ذلك] الإعياء إلى حول، وذلك مما لا معنى له . و [أيضا] قد بينا أمر الهجرة، وأنها كانت من الفرائض التي جاء في التخلف عنها الوعيد الشديد، ثم قد أثبت اسم الإيمان مع عدم ذلك. والله أعلم.
The verse about prayer, and what was mentioned of brotherhood and release, is obligatory by acceptance not by the act. Likewise, wasting occurs by rejection not by delay. And there is no power except with Allah. And Allah Most High said: 'And those who believed but did not emigrate, you have no guardianship over them until they emigrate' [al-Anfal 72], and He said: 'Do not say to those who give you peace, "You are not a believer"' [al-Nisa 94]; it is established that what he says does not remove faith nor its name. And Allah knows best. And he argued with His saying: 'If they repent and establish prayer' [al-Tawbah 5, 11] the verse, but we explained that that is regarding acceptance; if the act were awaited, brotherhood would never be obligatory, nor would they be released, and that would be exhaustion for a year, and that is meaningless. And also we have explained the matter of emigration, and that it was among the obligatory duties regarding which severe warning came for failing to perform, yet He affirmed the name of faith even with its absence. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 443

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the argument that abandoning actions removes faith. The author begins by explaining that the obligation of prayer and brotherhood is fulfilled by acceptance, not by the physical act. Similarly, 'wasting' occurs by rejection, not by delay. Then he cites two verses: one on emigration (al-Anfal 72) and one on greeting (al-Nisa 94), to prove that the name of faith remains even when emigration is not performed and even when someone is greeted with peace (indicating they are a believer). The opponent argued using the verse from Surat al-Tawbah about repentant polytheists who establish prayer, claiming that faith is conditional on action. The author counters that this verse is about acceptance of repentance, not about performing the act. If the act itself were required for brotherhood, it would never become obligatory and they would never be released, leading to an indefinite delay, which is meaningless. Finally, the author reminds that emigration was a serious obligation with severe threats for neglect, yet Allah still affirmed the name of faith for those who did not emigrate. Thus, neglecting actions does not remove faith.

Key Terms
الإيمان
الفعل
القبول
الهجرة
الإضاعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'If they repent and establish prayer' (al-Tawbah 5,11) makes faith conditional on performing prayer.
Correction: Maturidi explains that the verse is about acceptance of repentance, not the act itself. If the act were required for brotherhood, it would never become obligatory.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi argue about the verse 'If they repent and establish prayer'?
A) It proves that faith is conditional on performing prayer.
B) It is about acceptance of repentance, not the act.
C) It applies only to polytheists, not to believers.
D) It shows that faith is removed if prayer is abandoned.

Key Takeaway
Faith remains intact even when obligatory actions are not performed, as confirmed by Quranic verses and rational analysis.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Allah affirmed the name of faith for those who failed to emigrate despite the severe warning. How does this mercy impact your view of your own shortcomings? Now, ask yourself: Will you use this mercy as an excuse to continue neglecting, or as a motivation to finally act? What one concrete step will you take this week?

Three Interpretations of the Killing Verse
Maturidi enumerates three interpretations of the intentional killing verse to refute the Mu'tazilite proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi offers three distinct interpretations of the intentional killing verse to counter the Mu'tazilite argument and preserve the possibility of faith for a sinner.

Core Idea
A single verse can yield multiple valid interpretations that safeguard core doctrines.

The Mu'tazilites used the verse on intentional killing (Quran 4:93) to argue that a believer who commits murder loses faith entirely. Imam Maturidi counters by offering three alternative readings of the same verse, each showing that the verse does not necessarily entail removal of faith. In this lesson, we examine these three interpretations and how they protect the doctrine that a believer can commit major sins without leaving Islam.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
The Mu'tazilites cite the intentional killing verse as proof that a believer who kills loses faith. Maturidi refutes this by offering three alternative interpretations.
This is a classic kalam debate over the implications of a Qur'anic verse.
2
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the three aspects (أوجه) Maturidi lists for intentional killing.
3
Examine each interpretation
Go through each of the three interpretations: (1) killing for religion, (2) recompense with possibility of pardon, (3) verse refers to disbelievers. Understand how each undermines the Mu'tazilite claim.
Pay attention to the key terms: قتل العمد, الخطأ في القتل, الجزاء, العفو, الكفرة.
4
Reflect on the method
Notice how Maturidi uses tafsir (interpretation) to defend a theological point. This is a key method in kalam, showing that revelation supports orthodox doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج بقوله : ومن يقتل مؤمنا متعمدا الآية ، وقوله: يا أيها الذين آمنوا لا تأكلوا أموالكم بينكم بالباطل " الآية " ، وما ذُكر في أكل مال اليتيم " . فأما قتل العمد فله أوجه ثلاثة أحدها أن يكون فيمن تعمد القتل لدينه، وهذا أحد وجوه الخطأ في القتل. والله أعلم. والثاني أن يكون ذلك جزاؤه، ولله التفضل عليه بالعفو والمقابلة بالحسنات ولا قوة إلا بالله والثالث أن تكون الآية في الكفرة، وفي القصة دليل ذلك .
And he argued with His saying: 'And whoever kills a believer intentionally' the verse, and His saying: 'O you who believe, do not consume your wealth among yourselves unjustly' the verse, and what was mentioned about consuming the wealth of an orphan. As for intentional killing, it has three aspects: one is that it refers to one who intentionally kills for his religion, and this is one of the aspects of error in killing. And Allah knows best. The second is that that is his recompense, and Allah may graciously pardon him and exchange it with good deeds, and there is no power except with Allah. The third is that the verse concerns the disbelievers, and the story indicates that.
Source pages p. 443

Commentary
This passage begins by listing the opponent's proofs: the verse on intentional killing of a believer (al-Nisa 93), the verse on consuming wealth unjustly (al-Nisa 29-30), and the verse on orphan's wealth (al-Nisa 10). The author then directly addresses the killing verse, offering three possible interpretations to show that it does not prove removal of faith. The first interpretation: the verse refers to someone who intentionally kills another because of his religion (i.e., kills a believer for being a believer). This is considered a type of 'error in killing' (خطأ في القتل), suggesting something about the perpetrator's state. The second interpretation: the verse states the recompense (جزاء) for such killing, but Allah may graciously pardon the killer and reward him with good deeds instead. This leaves room for forgiveness. The third interpretation: the verse is actually about disbelievers (الكفرة), not about believers; the story (القصة) associated with the verse supports this. By providing these alternatives, the author undermines the opponent's claim that the verse necessarily removes faith from the killer.

Key Terms
قتل العمد
الخطأ في القتل
الجزاء
العفو
الكفرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might assume the verse applies universally to any believer who kills another, thus proving that the killer leaves the faith.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the verse may be specific to certain cases (killing for religion, or referring to disbelievers), and even if it applies, Allah may pardon the killer. Thus the verse does not definitively prove removal of faith.

Checkpoint
What is the first interpretation Maturidi gives for the verse on intentional killing?
A) It applies to anyone who kills intentionally.
B) It refers to someone who kills for his religion.
C) It is only about disbelievers.
D) It is about accidental killing.

Key Takeaway
Imam Maturidi demonstrates that a single Qur'anic verse can be interpreted in multiple ways to support key theological doctrines, effectively countering opponents' proof-texts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of someone you know who committed a major sin. Did you ever quietly assume they had lost their faith? How does Maturidi's teaching that the same verse can mean different things challenge you to hold back judgment? Will you consciously refrain from declaring anyone a disbeliever based on a single act?
?
The second interpretation says Allah may pardon and even exchange evil deeds for good ones. Is there a person you have given up on because of their repeated sins? What would it look like for you to hope for their pardon and treat them with mercy rather than condemnation? Decide on one concrete action this week that reflects such hope.
?
Maturidi suggests that some verses may be about disbelievers, not believers. When you hear a severe warning in the Quran, do you typically assume it applies to others? How would your life change if you personally received every warning as if it were addressed directly to you? Commit to reading one such verse this week as a personal message from Allah, not as a description of someone else.

The Qisas Verse: Retaining Faith and Brotherhood After Killing
Maturidi uses the qisas verse to prove retention of faith and brotherhood and refutes Mu'tazila interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the qisas verse to argue that a killer retains the name of faith and brotherhood, and how he refutes the Mu'tazila interpretation.

Core Idea
The qisas verse preserves the killer's faith and brotherhood, opposing eternal punishment.

Maturidi presents a crucial evidence from the Qur'anic verse on retaliation (al-Baqara 2:178). He points out that Allah addresses the believers and still calls the killer a 'brother,' showing that faith is not erased by sin. This passage also critiques the Mu'tazila for distorting the verse's meaning. Let's examine the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the flow.
2
Identify the key evidence
Notice how Maturidi extracts two key names: 'faith' (iman) and 'brotherhood' (ukhuwwa) from the verse.
3
Understand the Mu'tazila objection
See how the Mu'tazila reinterpret the verse to support their doctrine, and how Maturidi labels this as unjustified specification (takhsis).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم دليل ما بينا قوله تعالى: يا أيها الذين آمنوا كتب عليكم القصاص الآية، وإنما يكتب عليهم إذا قتلوا قتل العمد، وأبقى لهم بعد القتل اسم الإيمان؛ ثم قال : فمن عفى له من أخيه شيء فأبقى له اسم الأخوة. ثم قال : وذلك تخفيف من ربكم ورحمة أطمعه في رحمته جل وعلا، وبعيد أن يكون له مع هذا خلود في النار ثم رأى المعتزلة في ذلك إلزام القصاص بعد التوبة، وإزالة عقوبة الآخرة، وصرف الآية - وإن كان فيها ذكر الإيمان ـ إلى الخروج منه ؛ وذلك تخصيص.
Then the evidence for what we have clarified is His saying: 'O you who believe, retaliation is prescribed for you' the verse. He only prescribes it for them when they commit intentional killing, and He preserved for them after killing the name of faith. Then He said: 'So whoever is pardoned something by his brother' – so He preserved for him the name of brotherhood. Then He said: 'That is a lightening from your Lord and mercy' – He made him hope for His mercy, Exalted is He, and it is far that he would have eternal punishment in the Fire with this. Then the Mu'tazila saw in this an obligation of retaliation after repentance, and the removal of the punishment of the afterlife, and they turned the verse – even though it contains mention of faith – away from it; that is specification.
Source pages p. 444

Commentary
This passage provides an additional proof from the verse on qisas (al-Baqara 178). The author argues that the verse addresses believers ('O you who believe') and prescribes retaliation for intentional killing. Despite the killing, Allah preserved for them the name of faith after the killing. Moreover, the verse calls the killer a 'brother' ('from his brother'), preserving the name of brotherhood. The verse also says 'that is a lightening from your Lord and mercy,' giving hope for mercy. The author concludes that it is far-fetched that such a person would suffer eternal punishment (خلود في النار). Then the author criticizes the Mu'tazila for interpreting the verse to require retaliation after repentance (thereby removing punishment in the afterlife) and for turning the verse away from its apparent meaning (which includes mention of faith) to support their view. This, the author says, is a form of unjustified specification (تخصيص).

Key Terms
القصاص
اسم الإيمان
الأخوة
التخفيف
الرحمة
الخلود
التخصيص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The qisas verse only deals with legal punishment and has no theological implications about the killer's faith.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the verse preserves the names of faith and brotherhood for the killer, indicating that major sin does not remove faith or brotherhood.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does the qisas verse indicate about the killer's faith?
A) The killer loses faith immediately.
B) The killer retains the name of faith and brotherhood.
C) The killer must be executed without mercy.
D) The killer's faith is suspended until punishment.

Key Takeaway
The qisas verse proves that a Muslim who commits intentional killing remains a believer and a brother, and thus does not deserve eternal punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a Muslim who commits a grave sin, do you still consider them your brother or sister in faith? Or do you distance yourself, doubting their faith? How does this lesson challenge you to maintain brotherhood despite others' sins? Will you actively work to preserve that bond in your heart and actions starting today?
?
Have you ever felt that your sins are too great for Allah's mercy? This lesson shows that even an intentional killer is given hope through the verse. How does that reshape your own hope and repentance? Will you truly trust in Allah's mercy tonight and return to Him without despair?
?
The Mu'tazila twisted the clear verse to fit their system. Where in your life are you tempted to twist Allah's commands or reinterpret clear texts to justify your own desires or convenience? Will you commit to accepting the plain meaning of revelation, even when it is difficult or costly?

Specification of Wealth Verses
Maturidi specifies two Quranic verses on wealth to show they are not general proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that verses threatening punishment for consuming wealth unjustly are specific in their application, not general proofs against all sins.

Core Idea
Not every verse about sin is a general proof—some are specified to particular conditions.

Maturidi here addresses a potential objection: do general-sounding threats in the Quran prove that any sin removes faith? He analyzes two key verses on consuming wealth and shows they are specified by context, language, and qualifiers. This lesson unpacks his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the author's concise style.
2
Identify the two arguments
Maturidi gives two reasons for specification. First, the term 'consuming wealth' includes even a small amount, which would be too trivial for the severe punishment. Second, the verse includes qualifiers 'transgressively and unjustly', restricting its application.
3
Connect to the earlier discussion on killing
Maturidi mentions that this specification is similar to what was said about the verse on killing. Reflect on how that earlier analysis supports his current argument.
You may need to recall the previous lesson on killing.
4
Reflect on the principle of specification
Consider why specification (تخصيص) is important in Quranic interpretation. How does it prevent misapplication of verses?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما ذكر من أكل المال بالباطل فكل يُجمع على التخصيص، إذ ذلك اسم يأخذ القليل، وذلك غير مراد فيه ؛ وكذلك أموال اليتامى والثاني أنه ذكر فيه «عدوانًا وظلما»، وذلك على العدوان على حد الله والظلم على صاحبه، مع احتمال ذلك ما ذكرنا في القتل.
And what was mentioned about consuming wealth unjustly – all of it is agreed upon specification, since that term includes a small amount, and that is not intended in it. Likewise, the wealth of orphans. The second is that He mentioned in it 'transgressively and unjustly', which indicates transgression against Allah's boundaries and injustice against the owner, along with the possibility of that which we mentioned regarding killing.
Source pages p. 444

Commentary
This passage addresses the opponent's proof from the verse about consuming wealth unjustly (al-Nisa 29-30) and the verse about consuming orphan's wealth (al-Nisa 10). The author argues that these verses are understood to be specific (تخصيص) rather than general. The term 'consuming wealth' linguistically includes even a small amount, but the severe punishment mentioned in the verses is not intended for every trivial offense. Moreover, the verse on consuming wealth includes the qualifiers 'transgressively and unjustly' (عدوانًا وظلمًا), which restricts the meaning to transgression against Allah's limits and injustice against the owner. This specificity is similar to the earlier discussion on killing, where the verse might be limited in application. Thus, these verses cannot be used as general proofs that any sin removes faith.

Key Terms
أكل المال بالباطل
التخصيص
العدوان
الظلم
أموال اليتامى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the severe threat in these verses applies to any unjust consumption, no matter how small or under what circumstances.
Correction: Maturidi explains that the term 'consuming wealth' includes even a small amount, which is not the intended target of severe punishment. Additionally, the qualifiers 'transgressively and unjustly' restrict the verse to acts that combine both transgression against Allah's limits and injustice against the owner.

Checkpoint

A) Because the term includes even a small amount, which is not intended for severe punishment.
B) Because the verse was abrogated.
C) Because it only applies to polytheists.
D) Because it refers only to theft from Muslims.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that the apparent generality of Quranic threats can be limited by linguistic and contextual specification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Have you ever justified a small sin—like taking something insignificant that isn't yours—by thinking it's 'too minor' to matter? How does Maturidi's specification challenge that rationalization, knowing that the punishment is intended for severe transgressions?
?
The qualifiers 'transgressively and unjustly' restrict the verse, but also demand you examine both your relationship to Allah's boundaries and your treatment of others. Can you think of a specific situation where you violated both? How will you repent and change starting today?

Maturidi's Dilemma to the Mu'tazila on Faith
Maturidi challenges the Mu'tazila with a dilemma about verses mentioning faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's rhetorical dilemma against the Mu'tazila concerning verses that mention faith, and how it exposes an inconsistency in their position.

Core Idea
A single verse puts the Mu'tazila in an impossible choice: admit specification or join the Murji'a.

In this short but powerful passage, Imam Maturidi directly challenges his Mu'tazilite opponent with a dilemma that cuts to the heart of their theology. He focuses on any verse that mentions faith (like 'O you who believe') and asks: what do you do with that mention when the person addressed commits a sin? The answer forces the Mu'tazila to either concede they are arbitrarily specifying the verse or to accept a position they themselves condemn. Let's examine the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the direct tone—Maturidi is addressing an opponent directly.
The passage is a single sentence with a clear structure: question, two options, two consequences, and a closing phrase.
2
Identify the two options
The passage presents two possibilities regarding the verse that mentions faith: either remove the mention (interpret it away) or keep it as applying to the person. Write them down.
3
Examine the consequences
If you remove it, you admit specification (takhsis)—which the Mu'tazila reject without evidence. If you keep it, you agree with the Murji'a that faith does not decrease with sin—which the Mu'tazila also reject. Both options are unacceptable for them.
This shows the dilemma is designed to expose inconsistency.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends with 'And Allah is the grantor of success,' indicating that the argument is decisive. The Mu'tazila have no way out.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال له : الآية التي فيها ذكر الإيمان أتزيله أو تبقيه ؟ فإن أزاله فقد أقر بالتخصيص، وإن أبقاه رجع إلى رأي من نسبهم إلى الإرجاء. والله الموفق.
Then it is said to him: The verse which contains mention of faith – do you remove it or keep it? If you remove it, you have admitted specification. If you keep it, you return to the view of those who ascribe to irja'. And Allah is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 444

Commentary
This passage is a direct rhetorical challenge to the Mu'tazilite opponent (likely al-Ka'bi). The author refers to a verse that mentions faith (such as 'O you who believe' or similar). He presents a dilemma: either the Mu'tazila remove the mention of faith from the verse (i.e., interpret it as applying to non-believers or as not indicating true faith), in which case they admit they are committing specification (تخصيص) without justification; or they keep the mention of faith as applying to the person in question, in which case they would agree with the Murji'a (who hold that faith does not decrease with sins). Since the Mu'tazila oppose both specification without evidence and the Murji'a position, this dilemma exposes an inconsistency in their argument. The author concludes with 'والله الموفق' (Allah is the grantor of success).

Key Terms
الإيمان
التخصيص
الإرجاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The question refers to a specific known verse (e.g., 'O you who believe').
Correction: Maturidi's challenge is general: any verse that mentions faith applied to a person who later sins poses the same dilemma.

Checkpoint
What is the dilemma Maturidi presents to the Mu'tazila?
A) They must choose between accepting faith or rejecting paradise.
B) Either they remove the mention of faith (admitting specification) or keep it (agreeing with the Murji'a).
C) They must accept either eternal punishment or temporary punishment.
D) They must choose between literal and metaphorical interpretation.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that any verse attributing faith to a sinner creates an inescapable dilemma for the Mu'tazila: either they arbitrarily specify the verse or they join the Murji'a.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a verse of the Quran that challenges a belief you hold dear, do you have the courage to let the verse correct your understanding, even if it means admitting you were wrong? The Mu'tazila faced a similar choice—what is yours today?
?
This lesson reveals that the Mu'tazila's inconsistency arose from trying to reconcile their preconceived notions with clear revelation. Are there areas in your own life where you twist clear texts to fit your desires rather than submitting fully?
?
The dilemma ends with 'والله الموفق'—Allah grants success. In your struggle to align your heart with the truth, are you relying on Allah's guidance or on your own reasoning? What step can you take this week to seek His success in a matter you have been avoiding?

The Reality of Iman as Basis for Legal Consequences
Maturidi refutes using legal rulings and expiations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of the claim that legal consequences are based solely on the name of iman, and instead are grounded in its reality.

Core Idea
Legal rulings stem from the reality of faith, not just its name.

Maturidi engages with an opponent who argues that the name of iman should be denied to sinners because certain legal rulings are attached to it. He counters by questioning whether those rulings follow from the mere name or from the actual meaning and conditions of faith. This passage clarifies that even when punishments are applied, faith remains, and the punishments themselves are expiations that presuppose faith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's argument
Read the first sentence to see how the opponent uses legal rulings to argue that the name of iman should be prohibited for sinners.
The opponent links rulings to the name, not the reality.
2
Examine Maturidi's first rebuttal
Notice how Maturidi asks for evidence that rulings are due to the name alone, without meanings (ma'ani) connected to it.
This sets up the core distinction between name and reality.
3
Review the opponent's list and Maturidi's second question
The opponent lists veneration, purification, alliance, and acceptance of testimony. Maturidi again asks for proof that these are for the name alone, without realization through conditions.
4
Understand Maturidi's own explanation
Maturidi explains that the allegiance of faith is binding and that prohibitions are based on the faith that exists in the sinner. He uses the hadith about blood and wealth and the Quranic verse on pity to support his view.
Note how he argues that punishments are expiations, not signs of disbelief.
5
Grasp the final principle
Maturidi concludes that hudud and qisas are expiations precisely because faith has not left the person, unlike the punishments of kufr that lead to eternal fire.
This ties back to the reality of iman.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم استدل على منع اسم الإيمان بما جعل الله لما أطلق له اسم الإيمان أحكاما منعت منه، واسم الكفر أحكامًا والمن يقترن به. فيقال له: ما الدليل على أن الذي أُوصيت إليه من الأحكام لإطلاق دون معان تتصل به في الوجهين جميعا؟ ثم قال: منها التعظيم والتزكية والموالاة وقبول الشهادة. فيقال: ما الدلالة على أن كل هذا لإطلاق الاسم خاصة دون تحقيقه بالشرائط المضمومة إليه والعبادات التي دعا إليها الإيمان؟ وبعد، فإن ولاية الإيمان لازمة، وجميع ما منع منه منع بحق الإيمان الذي فيه؛ إذ حقيقته فيه منعنا عما ذكر مع ما كان الثابت مما فيه الحدود والقصاص يثبت له الولاية؛ وتجب له الشهادة وجميع ما ذكر، وتقبل توبته ويحد على ذلك؛ ولم يكن لأحد نفي الاسم عنه بما عفى عن عذاب الله، بل ذلك كله كما قال: فقد عصموا مني دماءهم وأموالهم إلا بحقها. ولا قوة إلا بالله وأيد ذلك قوله: ولا تأخذكم بهما رأفة في دين الله له؛ إنه لو كان الإيمان زائلاً عنه لكانت الرأفة لا تأخذه، بل رأفة الإيمان هي التي تأخذه حتى لعلها تبلغ إلى تعطيل الحد، فحذر ذلك. وأيد ذلك ما لو تاب. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وإقامة الحد من الرحمة، لأنه يكفره ويزيل عنه. والأصل أن عقوبات الكفر لا تطهر صاحبها بل تسلمه إلى عذاب الأبد، كقوله: أغرقوا فأدخلوا نارا. والحدود والقصاص جعلت كفارات، فثبت أنها جعلت كذلك لأنه لم يزل الإيمان عنه. والله أعلم.
Then he argued for prohibiting the name of faith (iman) by [pointing to] what God has made for those to whom the name of faith is applied – rulings that are prohibited from it, and the name of disbelief (kufr) – rulings that are associated with it. So it is said to him: What is the evidence that the rulings entrusted to you are [due] to the name alone without meanings connected to it in both cases? Then he said: Among them are veneration, purification, alliance, and acceptance of testimony. So it is said: What is the proof that all this is specifically for the name alone without its realization through the conditions attached to it and the acts of worship to which faith calls? Moreover, the allegiance of faith is necessary, and all that is prohibited from it is prohibited by right of the faith that is in him; since its reality is in him, it prevented us from what was mentioned, along with the fact that what is established including prescribed legal punishments and retaliation establishes for him allegiance, and testimony is obligatory for him, and all that was mentioned, and his repentance is accepted, and he is flogged for that. And it was not for anyone to deny the name from him because of the remission of God's punishment, rather all that is as he said: 'They have protected from me their blood and wealth except by its right.' And there is no power except through God. And that is supported by His saying: 'And let not pity for them seize you in the religion of God' – for if faith had departed from him, pity would not seize him, but rather the pity of faith is what seizes him, until it might reach the point of suspending the prescribed punishment, so He warned against that. And that is supported by what if he repents. And there is no power except through God. And the execution of the prescribed punishment is from mercy, because it expiates [the sin] and removes it from him. And the principle is that the punishments of disbelief do not purify their doer but rather deliver him to eternal punishment, as His saying: 'They were drowned and entered a Fire.' And the prescribed punishments and retaliation were made expiations, so it is established that they were made so because faith has not left him. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 445

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer sequence. The opponent argues that the name 'iman' should be prohibited for the sinner because God attached certain rulings to the name of iman and kufr. Maturidi responds by asking: what evidence is there that these rulings are due to the name alone and not due to the actual meaning (haqiqa) connected to it in both cases? The opponent lists rulings: veneration, purification, alliance, acceptance of testimony. Maturidi again asks: what proof that these are for the name alone, without the conditions and acts of worship that faith entails? He then explains that the allegiance of faith is binding, and all prohibitions are based on the faith that exists in him. Since the reality of faith is in him, it prevents us from those prohibitions. He continues: even when legal punishments (hudud) and retaliation are established, the person still has allegiance and his testimony is accepted, and his repentance is accepted, and he is flogged for the sin. No one can deny the name from him because of the remission of eternal punishment. He cites the hadith: 'They have protected from me their blood and wealth except by its right' (i.e., the right of the law). He then supports this with the Quranic verse: 'Let not pity for them seize you in the religion of God' (24:2). Maturidi reasons: if faith had entirely left such a person, then pity would not hold back the punishment, but the pity that comes from faith might cause suspension of the punishment, so God warned against it. The execution of the prescribed punishment is a mercy because it expiates the sin and removes its burden from him. In contrast, the punishments of disbelief do not purify; they lead to eternal punishment, as in the story of Pharaoh's people. Therefore, the fact that hudud and qisas are expiations proves that faith has not left the sinner.

Key Terms
الإيمان
الكفر
الحدود
القصاص
الولاية
الشهادة
التعظيم
التزكية
الموالاة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the application of legal punishments implies the person has lost faith entirely.
Correction: Maturidi shows that punishments are expiations that require the continued presence of faith, not its absence.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the evidence that legal punishments (hudud) are expiations (kaffarat) for the sinner?
A) They are the same as punishments for kufr.
B) They cleanse the sinner from sin and do not deliver him to eternal punishment.
C) They are only applied to those who have lost faith.
D) They are a sign of God's anger.

Key Takeaway
Legal punishments are expiations that confirm the continued reality of faith in the sinner, not its absence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson teaches that even when a Muslim commits a major sin and faces a legal punishment, the reality of iman remains. When you think of your own sins, do you truly believe that your faith is still intact, or do you sometimes feel that God has rejected you entirely? What would change in your life if you took seriously that even your failures do not erase the faith within you?
?
Maturidi argues that hudud are a mercy because they expiate sins. Are you willing to accept the difficult consequences of your wrongdoings—whether from the law or from personal accountability—as a means of purification, or do you try to avoid them? How does your attitude toward punishment reflect your understanding of God’s mercy?
?
You may have judged someone who falls into repeated sin as having 'lost their iman' or being a hypocrite. Based on this passage, what right do you have to deny the name of faith from a person whom God has not denied? How can you balance compassion with upholding the truth that faith is still present even in the struggling believer?

Faith and Disbelief
intermediate
6 min
No Intermediate Between Faith and Disbelief
Maturidi refutes the idea of a third category between iman and kufr in names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that there is no third category between faith and disbelief, and that claiming such is an innovation in religion.

Core Idea
In Islam, people are either believers or disbelievers—there is no middle ground.

This passage from Imam Maturidi directly challenges the Mu'tazilite concept of a middle status (al-manzila bayn al-manzilatayn). Maturidi argues that God Himself divided humanity into only two categories in both this world and the Hereafter. Any addition to this division is an unwarranted innovation. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the full argument in Maturidi's own words.
Notice the rhetorical structure: objection followed by assertion.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent posits three divisions: rulings of kufr, iman, and a third intermediate. Locate where Maturidi presents this view.
This is the Mu'tazilite position that he refutes.
3
See how Maturidi challenges the evidence
Maturidi asks for the proof (dalil) for an intermediate category in names. He does not accept it as self-evident.
4
Understand his alternative division
Maturidi asserts that God divided rational humans into only two categories. Find the basis for this claim.
Refer to Quran 64:2 as implied by the division of mankind.
5
Reflect on the warning against innovation
He adds that anyone who adds a third category is an innovator, supported by a hadith about the curse on those who shelter innovators.
This reinforces the severity of such an addition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول: إذ الله يقسم الأحكام أقساما ثلاثة من حيث الانقسام من اسم الكفر، والإيمان، وما ليس بكفر ولا إيمان؛ حتى إذا زال حكم ذينك عنه لزم الواسط، فما الدليل على أن ثمة واسطاً في الأسماء؟ بل الله قسم في الجملة البشر المحتمل للعلم قسمين في أمر الدنيا والآخرة جميعاً، فمن زاد عليه فهو المبدع في دين الله ما لم يؤذن له، وقد قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم من آوى محدثاً فعليه لعنة ، فكيف بمن هو الذي أحدث الحدث. فنسأل الله العصمة.
Then we say: Since God divides the rulings into three divisions in terms of division from the name of disbelief, faith, and what is neither disbelief nor faith; so that when the ruling of those two departs from him, the intermediate (al-wasit) applies. So what is the evidence that there is an intermediate (wasit) in the names? Rather, God has divided mankind who are capable of knowledge into two divisions in the matter of this world and the Hereafter altogether. Whoever adds to this is an innovator (mubdi') in the religion of God without His permission. And the Messenger of God (peace be upon him) said: 'Whoever gives refuge to an innovator, upon him is the curse.' So how about the one who himself is the innovator? So we ask God for protection.
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the concept of an intermediate category (al-manzila bayn al-manzilatayn) claimed by the Mu'tazila. Maturidi states that the opponent posits three divisions of rulings: those pertaining to the name of kufr, those pertaining to the name of iman, and those that are neither. The opponent argues that when the ruling of the first two (iman and kufr) is removed from a person, a third intermediate ruling applies. Maturidi challenges: what evidence is there for an intermediate category in the names? He then asserts that God has divided rational human beings into only two categories in both worldly and otherworldly matters, referencing Quran 64:2. Anyone who adds a third category is an innovator (mubdi') in religion without God's permission. He reinforces this with a hadith: those who give refuge to an innovator are cursed – how much more so the innovator himself.

Key Terms
الواسط
الأسماء
الأحكام
الكفر
الإيمان
المبدع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the concept of a middle status (al-manzila bayn al-manzilatayn) is a valid theological position.
Correction: Maturidi argues it is an innovation with no scriptural basis; God divided humans into only two categories (believer and disbeliever) in this world and the Hereafter.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi respond to the claim that there is an intermediate category of ruling between iman and kufr?
A) He accepts it as a valid deduction from the Quran.
B) He argues that God only divides people into two categories and any addition is innovation.
C) He says it is a matter of scholarly disagreement.
D) He remains silent on the issue.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi rejects any intermediate category between faith and disbelief, grounding his argument in God's division of humans into only two groups and warning that adding a third is an innovation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there an area of your life where you are trying to maintain a comfortable middle ground—partly submitting to Allah and partly following your desires? What step will you take today to leave that middle and commit fully to faith?
?
The hadith warns that giving refuge to an innovator brings a curse. Could you be tolerating innovations in your own worship out of habit or cultural comfort? How will you honestly identify and remove them to avoid falling into that warning?
?
If Allah has divided humanity into only two groups—believers and disbelievers—then where do you truly stand in the hidden corners of your heart? What concrete action will you take to ensure that your faith is not merely a label but a reality that separates you from disbelief?

Faith and Disbelief
beginner
4 min
The Quranic Threefold Division Refutes a Middle Status
Maturidi refutes a middle status using the Quran's three categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi refutes the concept of a middle status (al-wasit) by appealing to the Quran's explicit threefold classification of people.

Core Idea
The Quran divides humanity into three: believers, disbelievers, and hypocrites – no fourth category.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi addresses a critical theological debate: does the Quran allow for a 'middle status' between belief and unbelief? He answers with a firm no, grounding his argument in the Quran's own categories. This lesson will unpack his reasoning and show why any invented intermediary disrupts the divine order.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Maturidi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the three Quranic divisions
The passage lists three groups: disbelievers, believers, and hypocrites. Find each one and note how the hypocrites are described as 'wavering'.
3
Understand Maturidi's argument
The passage argues that anyone who tries to establish an intermediary not based on the text has wasted the rights of division and disrupted the Quranic order. Ask yourself why this is a strong refutation.
This connects to the learning objective.
4
Reflect on the consequences
Consider the consequence: such a person deserves wrath from all Muslims. This shows the gravity of the issue.
The closing formula 'la hawla wa la quwwata illa billah' is a common expression of reliance on Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما كان الله تعالى قد بين الأقسام الثلاثة الكفرة والمؤمنين وأهل النفاق، وهم المذبذبون بينهما، وأخبر أنهم ليسوا من هؤلاء ولا من هؤلاء . فمن رام تثبيت الواسط، لا على ما جاء به النص، وأراد أن يجعله مقابلاً له، على نفي الحقيقة التي جعل الله لها الواسط، فقد ضيع حقوق القسمة ونقض الترتيب الذي جاء به القرآن فاستوجب المقت به من جميع منتحلي الإسلام. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Allah Almighty has made clear the three divisions: the disbelievers, the believers, and the hypocrites, who are wavering between them, and He informed that they are not of these nor of those. So whoever seeks to establish an intermediary not based on what the text brought, and wants to make it a counterpart to the text, negating the reality for which Allah made the intermediary, has certainly wasted the rights of division and disrupted the order brought by the Qur'an, thus deserving wrath from all those who profess Islam. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the idea of a 'middle status' (al-wāsiṭ) for the grave sinner. Maturidi begins by stating that Allah has clearly divided people into three divisions: disbelievers (kuffār), believers (mu'minīn), and hypocrites (ahl al-nifāq). The hypocrites are described as 'wavering between them' (mudhabdhabīn), not being from either group. He then argues that anyone who tries to establish an intermediary not based on the revealed text, making it a rival to the divine categories, has wasted the rights of division (huqūq al-qisma) and disrupted the order of the Qur'an. Such a person incurs wrath (al-maqt) from all Muslims. The passage closes with the formula 'lā ḥawla wa lā quwwata illā billāh'.

Key Terms
الأقسام الثلاثة
الكفرة
المؤمنين
أهل النفاق
المذبذبون
الواسط
حقوق القسمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: There is a middle status (al-wasit) between belief and unbelief that is recognized by the Quran.
Correction: The Quran only recognizes three categories: believers, disbelievers, and hypocrites. Hypocrites are not a middle but a distinct third group; any other intermediary is a human invention.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does the Quran's threefold division consist of?
A) Believers, unbelievers, and grave sinners
B) Believers, unbelievers, and hypocrites
C) Muslims, Jews, and Christians
D) The righteous, the wicked, and the average

Key Takeaway
The Quran's threefold division (believers, disbelievers, hypocrites) leaves no room for an invented middle status; any claim to such a status disrupts the divine order and incurs condemnation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage says that anyone who tries to establish an intermediary not based on the text incurs wrath from all Muslims. Are there any beliefs or practices you have adopted that are not clearly based on the Quran and Sunnah? How would you respond to this warning today?
?
The hypocrites are described as 'wavering' between the believers and disbelievers. In what areas of your life are you wavering between obedience to Allah and following your desires? What commitment can you make to be firmly among the believers?
?
Maturidi states that disrupting the Quranic order invites divine wrath. Have you ever prioritized your own reasoning or cultural norms over a clear Quranic category (e.g., regarding faith, disbelief, or hypocrisy)? What would it take for you to submit fully to the divine classification?

Faith and Disbelief
beginner
4 min
Maturidi Dismisses the Objection from the Example of a Woman
Maturidi dismisses an objection about the exceptional ruling for women.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi dismisses the objection from the example of a woman as irrelevant for theological argumentation.

Core Idea
Rulings of disbelief are diverse; specific cases don't disprove general principles.

In this passage, Maturidi confronts an objection raised by his opponent (likely al-Ka'bi) who uses the example of a woman committing a major sin as an exception. Maturidi dismisses this as mere fancy and explains that the rulings of disbelief are varied and cannot serve as universal evidence. He then criticizes the opponent's ineffective refutations of the Khawarij.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the dismissive tone in the opening phrase.
2
Identify the objection
Find the opponent's claim about the woman. He uses it as an exception to argue against Maturidi's position.
The objection is rooted in a specific case of a woman committing a major sin.
3
Analyze Maturidi's response
Maturidi rejects the objection by stating that rulings of disbelief are diverse and cannot be used as evidence. He then critiques the opponent's prolonged refutations of the Khawarij as ineffective.
Understand why Maturidi says the opponent did not achieve his goal.
4
Reflect on the broader principle
Consider why theological arguments must rely on general principles, not isolated cases. Maturidi emphasizes the diversity in rulings of disbelief.
This lesson shows a key method of theological reasoning: avoiding false analogies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عارض نفسه بالمرأة / فزعم أنها مخصوص. وهذا النوع من الخيال، بل أحكام الكفر مختلفة لا يستدل بها على شيء. وقد رأيته أطنب في معارضات الخوارج، وتكلف الخروج مما قابلوه به مما يعلم كل من تأمله أنه لم يحقق ما رام إلزامه، ولا تخلّص مما قوبل به حق التخلص، فأغضيت عن ذكرها .
Then he objected with [the example of] the woman, and claimed that she is an exception. But this is a type of fancy; rather, the rulings of disbelief are diverse and cannot be used as evidence for anything. And I have seen him elaborate in his objections against the Khawarij, and exert himself to get out of what they confronted him with, such that anyone who examines it knows that he did not achieve what he aimed to impose, nor did he extricate himself properly from what he was confronted with, so I refrained from mentioning them.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The opponent (likely al-Ka'bi) tried to use the example of a woman who commits a major sin as an exception to the general ruling. Maturidi dismisses this as mere fantasy (al-khayāl), arguing that the rulings of disbelief (aḥkām al-kufr) are varied and cannot be used as general evidence. He then criticizes the opponent's lengthy refutations of the Khawarij as ineffective: the opponent did not achieve his aim nor properly extricate himself. Therefore, Maturidi says he refrains from mentioning those refutations in detail.

Key Terms
عارض نفسه
المرأة
مخصوص
أحكام الكفر
الخوارج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a specific case (e.g., women) disproves a general rule, but Maturidi points out that rulings for disbelief are not uniform.
Correction: The diversity in rulings of disbelief means that isolated examples cannot be used to contest general theological principles.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi respond to the opponent's objection about the woman?
A) He agrees and modifies the position.
B) He says the rulings of disbelief are diverse and cannot be used as evidence.
C) He says women are not accountable for major sins.
D) He ignores the objection.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi argues that the diversity in rulings of disbelief means that isolated examples cannot be used to contest general theological principles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a clear truth or command from Allah, do you ever search for a single exception or special case to excuse yourself from following it? What is one example where you have done this, and what would it take for you to instead submit fully without looking for loopholes?
?
Maturidi refrained from engaging with arguments he deemed unproductive. Are you willing to stop defending positions that are weak or self-serving, even if it means losing face? What conversation or debate do you need to walk away from for the sake of truth?
?
The opponent used the example of a woman to make a point. Have you ever used your own circumstances—gender, age, background, or past mistakes—to justify not living up to a responsibility? How can you stop treating yourself as a 'special case' and fully embrace what Allah requires of you?
